Shirkah — Partnership
beginner
6 min
Definition and Types of Shirkah: Milk and Aqd
Definition and classification of shirkah into milk and aqd with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define shirkah, distinguish between shirkat al-milk and shirkat al-`aqd, and state the proofs for its permissibility.

Core Idea
Partnership in Hanafi law is either co-ownership (milk) or contractual (aqd); only the latter requires agency-capable subject.

This lesson introduces the foundational definitions of shirkah (partnership) in Hanafi fiqh. The text distinguishes two primary types: shirkat al-milk (joint ownership) which arises without a contract, and shirkat al-`aqd (contractual partnership) which requires offer, acceptance, and a subject eligible for agency. Understanding this distinction is crucial because the legal rulings—especially regarding permission to transact—differ sharply between the two.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
From the passage, note that shirkah is divided into shirkat al-milk (ownership partnership) and shirkat al-`aqd (contractual partnership). Understand that the first arises from events like inheritance or purchase, the second from a contract.
The term 'shirkah' linguistically means mixing, and legally it is a contract that mixes shares indistinguishably.
2
Examine shirkat al-milk
Shirkat al-milk is co-ownership of a specific asset without a partnership contract. Each owner is a stranger to the other's share; no agency exists unless explicitly granted.
Selling one's share to an outsider is generally permissible except when the asset is mixed beyond easy distinction, requiring the partner's consent.
3
Examine shirkat al-`aqd
Shirkat al-`aqd is formed by offer and acceptance (ijab wa qabul). Its validity requires that the subject matter be capable of agency (wakalah), so each partner acts as principal for himself and agent for the other.
This condition excludes mubahat (free goods) like firewood or grass, because agency cannot be established over unowned items.
4
Review the proofs
The permissibility of partnership in general is established by Quran (4:12, 4:29), hadith ('I am the third of the two partners'), ijma of the scholars, and reason (as a means to seek lawful provision).
The hadith also warns that betrayal (khianah) removes divine blessing but does not invalidate the contract itself.

[هو مصدر شركت الرجل أشركه شركة في المال، وشريك الرجل ومشاركه سواء، وفي الشرع: عبارة عن عقد اختلاط النصيبين فصاعداً لا يفرق أحد النصيبين عن الآخر. (عيني، فتح) شركة الملك: أي الشركة على نوعين: الأول: شركة الملك. (عيني) اثنان: التقييد بالاثنين اتفاقي؛ لأن الحكم في الثلاثة فصاعداً لا يختلف. (معدن) إرثاً: أي من حيث الإرث بأن ورثاها من مورث (عيني) أو شراء أي من حيث الشراء بأن اشترياها أو هبة بأن الهباها، أو ملكاها بالاستيلاء، أو اختلط مالهما أحد، أو خلطاه خلطاً بحيث يعسر التميز أو يتعذر فكل هذا شركة ملك. (فتح) وشركة العقد: أي النوع الثاني من الشركة شركة العقد وركنها الإيجاب والقبول، كما أشار إليه بقوله: "أن يقول أحدهما" إلخ، وشرطها: أن يكون فيما يقبل الوكالة، ليكون كل واحد منهما في الشراء أصيلاً في نصفه ووكيلاً من صاحبه في النصف الآخر، فيكون المشترى مشتركاً بينهما، ويكون الكسب كذلك بحسب الشركة، فلا يجوز في الاحتطاب والاحتشاش ونحوهما من المباحات؛ لأن التوكيل لا يصح فيه [رمز الحقائق: ٤٦٤/١] (مستخلص)
The book of partnership, i.e., explaining its rulings. Shirkat al-Milk: that two people own a specific thing by inheritance or purchase, and each is a stranger in the share of his partner. Shirkat al-Aqd: that one of them says 'I have partnered with you.' i.e., it is that one of the two says to the other. [It is the verbal noun of 'sharaka' (to share), and a partner (sharik) and a participant (musharik) are the same. Legally, it is a contract of mixing of shares and above without distinguishing one share from the other. (Ayni, Ma'din)] The two books correspond in that both involve mixing. And 'shirk' (with kasr of shin and sukun of ra') means share, as the Prophet ﷺ said: 'Whoever frees a share (shirkan) he has in a slave,' i.e., a portion. It is established by the Book: Allah says: "Then they are partners in the third" (4:12); and by his saying ﷺ: 'Allah says: I am the third of the two partners as long as they do not betray each other; if they betray, I depart from between them'; and by consensus, for the imams have agreed on its permissibility; and by reason, for it is a means to seek bounty, which is legislated by Allah's saying: "that you seek provision with your wealth" (4:29). Linguistically, shirkah means mixing of shares; then it is used legally for the contract because the contract is the cause of mixing, so the legal meaning is more specific. Its pillar in the contract is the mixing. Its condition of permissibility: that each party is capable of partnership. Its ruling: that the collection from the two shares becomes shared between them. (Ayni, Fath) Shirkat al-Milk: i.e., partnership is of two types: the first is shirkat al-milk. (Ayni) The restriction to two is incidental, for the ruling for three and above does not differ. (Ma'din) 'By inheritance': meaning from the deceased (Ayni) 'or purchase': meaning by buying, or gift, or possession by conquest, or if their properties mix and become indistinguishable; all this is shirkat al-milk. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/464] His saying 'each stranger in the share of his partner' because it does not involve agency; in some copies 'in the share of other than him,' and the first is more appropriate because 'other than him' includes even one with whom he has no partnership, unlike the expression 'partner'. It is not permissible for one of them to transact in it except with the partner's permission. If he sells his share to his partner, it is valid in any case; likewise if he sells to someone else except in the case of mixing, it is not permissible to sell to a stranger except with the partner's consent. (Fath) Shirkat al-Aqd: the second type of partnership, its pillar is offer and acceptance, as indicated by his saying 'that one says.' Its condition: that it be in something that permits agency, so that each of them in purchase is a principal in his half and an agent for his partner in the other half, so that the purchased item is shared between them, and the profit is likewise according to the partnership. It is not permissible in firewood gathering, grass gathering, and the like from mubahat, because agency is not valid in them. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/464] (Mustakhlas)
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on partnership (shirkah) defines the two main types. First, shirkat al-milk (joint ownership) arises without a contract, such as inheritance or joint purchase, where each co-owner is a stranger to the other's share and cannot transact without permission. Second, shirkat al-`aqd (contractual partnership) is formed by offer and acceptance, and its validity depends on the subject being eligible for agency. This is because each partner acts as principal for himself and agent for the other. Consequently, partnership is not valid in gathering freely available goods (mubah) like firewood and grass, because agency cannot be established over something that is not yet owned. The text also provides evidence from the Quran, Sunnah, ijma, and reason for the permissibility of partnership in general, and then details the conditions and rulings of both types.

Key Terms
شركة (shirkah): Legally, a contract that mixes shares such that they become indistinguishable.
شركة الملك (shirkat al-milk): Co-ownership of a specific asset by inheritance, purchase, gift, etc., without a partnership contract.
شركة العقد (shirkat al-`aqd): A contractual partnership formed by offer and acceptance, where each partner is an agent for the other in transactions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is shirkat al-`aqd not valid in gathering firewood (ihtitab)?
A) Because firewood is impure
B) Because agency is not valid over mubah (free goods)
C) Because it requires a specific place
D) Because only two people cannot gather firewood

Key Takeaway
Shirkat al-`aqd requires an explicit contract and a subject capable of agency; shirkat al-milk is mere co-ownership without mutual agency.

Shirkah — Partnership
beginner
8 min
Definition and Types of Partnership: Co-ownership vs Contractual Partnership
Defines shirkah and divides into co-ownership and contractual partnership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between co-ownership (shirkat al-milk) and contractual partnership (shirkat al-'aqd) and state the key legal implications of each.

Core Idea
In co-ownership, partners cannot act without permission; in contractual partnership, agency is built-in.

This lesson unpacks the foundational division of partnership in Hanafi fiqh. The text defines shirkah linguistically and legally, then separates it into two types: co-ownership of a specific asset and contractual partnership based on offer and acceptance. Understanding this distinction is crucial because the legal consequences differ significantly—especially regarding the authority to dispose of the shared property.

Guided Path
1
Linguistic and legal definition
Understand that shirkah linguistically means mixing shares, and legally it is a contract of mixing shares that become indistinguishable. Note that the legal meaning is more specific because the contract is the cause.
The term is used for both the contract and the resulting co-ownership.
2
Identify the two types
Shirkat al-Milk (co-ownership) arises from inheritance, purchase, gift, etc., without a formal contract. Shirkat al-'Aqd (contractual partnership) is formed by offer and acceptance.
The restriction to two partners is incidental; the same rules apply for three or more.
3
Determine agency
In co-ownership, each partner is a stranger to the other's share—no agency is implied. In contractual partnership, each partner acts as both principal and agent for the other.
This is the most critical difference.
4
Apply the ruling on disposal
In co-ownership, a partner cannot dispose of the asset without the other's consent, except when selling to the partner himself. For thoroughly mixed goods, even selling to a third party requires consent.
For contractual partnership, the scope of agency is defined by the contract.
5
Check conditions for contractual partnership
The subject matter must be capable of agency. Partnerships to gather firewood or grass are invalid because agency cannot apply to such common property.
This is a Hanafi-specific condition.

The Book of Partnership: Rulings of Partnership. The Book of Partnership means explaining its rulings. Co-ownership: that two individuals own a specific property by inheritance or purchase, and each is a stranger regarding the share of his partner. Contractual partnership: that one of them says: 'I have partnered with you'... The Book of Partnership: [It is derived from 'sharaka' meaning he made him a partner in wealth, and 'sharik' and 'musharik' are the same. Legally: it is a contract of mixing two or more shares such that one share cannot be distinguished from the other. (Ayni, Ma'din)]... Partnership is proven by the Book (Quran): 'They are partners in the third' (4:12), and by his statement (peace be upon him): 'Allah says: I am the third of the two partners as long as one does not betray the other. If he betrays, I depart from them.' And by consensus of the imams on its permissibility. And by reason: it is a means of seeking bounty, which is legislated by His statement: 'that you seek with your wealth' (4:24). Linguistically, partnership means mixing of shares. Then legally this name is applied to the contract of partnership, as the contract is the cause of mixing. Thus the legal meaning is more specific than the linguistic. Its pillar in the contract is mixing. Its condition of permissibility: that the property be capable of partnership. Its ruling: that the combined property from the two shares becomes jointly owned between them. Co-ownership: partnership is of two types: first, co-ownership... (Ayni). The restriction to two is incidental, because the ruling for three or more does not differ. (Ma'din) 'By inheritance' meaning from an estate, 'or purchase' meaning by buying, or gift, or acquisition by conquest, or their wealth mixed, or they mixed it such that distinguishing becomes difficult or impossible – all this is co-ownership. His statement: 'each is a stranger regarding the share of his partner' because it does not include agency. In some copies 'regarding the share of another' – the first is better because 'another' includes cases where the other is not a partner, unlike 'partner' which specifies that he is his partner. Neither may dispose of it without the permission of his partner. If he sells his share to his partner, it is permissible in any case. Likewise if he sells it to someone else, except in the case of mixing (khalṭ and ikhtilāṭ); then he may not sell it to a stranger except with the consent of his partner. (Fath) Contractual partnership: the second type of partnership, contractual partnership. Its pillar is offer and acceptance, as indicated by his statement: 'one of them says' etc. Its condition is that it be in something that accepts agency, so that each in purchasing is an agent for himself regarding half and an agent for his partner regarding the other half; thus what is purchased becomes shared between them, and earnings likewise according to the partnership. Hence it is not valid in gathering firewood, grass, and similar common property, because agency is not valid in them. (Ramz al-Haqā'iq 1/464) (Mukhtaṣar)... The Book of Partnership: Rulings of Partnership. ... and the other accepts – it is Mufāwaḍah if it includes agency and surety, and they are equal in capital, actions, and debt. So it is not valid between a free person and a slave, meaning regarding actions... Mufāwaḍah partnership, from the two partners. The other accepts by saying 'I accept', because it is a contract, so offer and acceptance are necessary. It is Mufāwaḍah: The author, following the author of al-Hidāyah, made contractual partnership into four types: Mufāwaḍah, ʿInān, Taqabbul, and Wujūh. But there is a contention, because it implies that partnership of crafts (ṣanāʾiʿ) and credit (wujūh) are distinct from Mufāwaḍah and ʿInān, which is not the case. The better division is that of al-Ṭaḥāwī and others: contractual partnership is of three kinds: by capital, labor, and credit; each can be Mufāwaḍah or ʿInān. Mufāwaḍah is from tafwīḍ meaning equality in everything. This contract is named thus because equality is required in all respects. His statement: 'if it includes agency and surety' – as for the first, because partnership is realized as we explained. As for the second, because equality is realized, for Mufāwaḍah demands it. It is said 'fāwaḍa' meaning he made equal. So equality must be realized at the beginning and end, meaning each of the two partners is an agent for the other in trade operations and their adjuncts, and a guarantor for the other regarding trade liabilities and their appendages like hiring, transport, laundering, and dyeing. Thus each partner is liable due to the trade of the other. If you say: agency for an unknown thing is not permissible, so this partnership should be invalid? I say: agency for the unknown is not intended directly; it is implicit. Hence Muḍārabah is valid despite ignorance, because it is agency to buy something unknown within the contract. The same applies here. If you say: surety (kafālah) is not valid except with the acceptance of the guarantee in the session, so how is it valid here when the guarantee is unknown? I say: that is regarding surety intended directly; but if it enters as part of another contract, it is not required, as in agency. (Ayni, Fath) 'In capital' meaning regarding capital and profit. (Ayni) 'And debt' meaning regarding debt, because Mufāwaḍah indicates equality, and that is only with what is mentioned. (Ayni) So it is not valid between a free person and a slave, because a free adult has capacity for disposition and surety, whereas a slave has none of that except with the permission of his master. His statement: 'and a minor' – meaning Mufāwaḍah is not valid between a minor and an adult, because the minor cannot dispose except with the master's permission, and cannot provide surety even with permission. His statement: 'and a Muslim' and 'a non-Muslim' – meaning it is not valid between a Muslim and a non-Muslim according to the two (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad), but Abū Yūsuf allowed it despite difference of religion, e.g., if a Muslim and a dhimmī contract Mufāwaḍah, because what the dhimmī may own in buying wine and pork, the Muslim can own by appointing someone else as agent; thus equality is realized, but it is disliked. According to the two, it is not valid and the partnership becomes ʿInān, because the dhimmī owns that himself while the Muslim does not, so equality is missing. Furthermore, Mufāwaḍah is not valid except with the word 'Mufāwaḍah' or by explicit mention of all its requirements, because most people do not know its conditions; thus it must be explicitly stated or its implications mentioned so that it is clear and apparent. For example, one says to the other, both being free, adult, Muslim: 'I have partnered with you in all the cash I own, on the basis of general delegation from each of us to the other in trades, cash, and credit, on condition that each of us is guarantor for the other regarding whatever is necessary from any sale.' And in every place where Mufāwaḍah is not valid =
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Partnership (Kitāb al-Shirkah) in the Hanafi fiqh manual. It begins by defining partnership linguistically (mixing of shares) and legally (a contract of mixing shares that become indistinguishable). The author establishes the legitimacy of partnership from four sources: the Quran (4:12, where heirs are called partners), the Hadith Qudsi where Allah declares Himself the third partner as long as there is no betrayal, scholarly consensus (ijma), and rational reasoning (ma'qul) since partnership is a means of seeking lawful provision, which is encouraged in the Quran (4:24). The legal meaning is more specific than the linguistic because the contract is the cause of the mixing. The pillar (rukn) in a contractual partnership is the mixing itself, and its condition is that the property be capable of being subject to partnership. The ruling is that the combined property becomes jointly owned. The author then distinguishes two types: 'Shirkat al-Milk' (co-ownership) and 'Shirkat al-'Aqd' (contractual partnership). In co-ownership, two or more individuals come to own a specific asset through inheritance, purchase, gift, conquest, or accidental mixing where shares cannot be distinguished. Importantly, neither partner can dispose of the asset (e.g., sell it to a third party) without the consent of the other, unless selling to the partner himself. However, in cases where the goods have been thoroughly mixed (khalṭ or ikhtilāṭ), even selling to a third party requires mutual consent. This is because co-ownership does not inherently include agency; each partner is a stranger to the other's share. The second type is contractual partnership (Shirkat al-'Aqd), whose pillar is offer and acceptance. Its condition is that the subject matter must be something in which agency is valid, so that each partner acts as both principal (for his own half) and agent (for his partner's half). Thus they can jointly purchase and share profits. This type is not valid for gathering firewood or grass from open lands, as agency cannot be performed for such common properties. The passage then delves into the sub-type of Mufāwaḍah (universal partnership). Mufāwaḍah requires the inclusion of both agency (wakāla) and surety (kafāla) between the partners, and full equality in three areas: capital, actions (authority to trade and manage), and debt liabilities. The reason is that Mufāwaḍah literally means equality, and the contract is built upon it. The author addresses potential objections: agency for an unknown thing is normally invalid, but here it is implicit within the partnership contract—similar to the validity of Muḍārabah despite ignorance of the specific purchase. Similarly, surety normally requires explicit acceptance of the beneficiary in the session, but when it is part of a larger contract, it is valid by implication. Mufāwaḍah is invalid between a free person and a slave because a slave lacks capacity for independent disposition and surety, even with the master's permission. It is also invalid between a minor and an adult for the same reason. A notable intra-Hanafi dispute is presented: Abū Yūsuf allowed Mufāwaḍah between a Muslim and a non-Muslim dhimmī, arguing that the Muslim can own wine and pork through an agent, thus achieving legal equality, though he considered it makruh (disliked). However, the two (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad, or in some texts Abū Yūsuf's own later view?) According to the preferred Hanafi position, Abū Ḥanīfa and Muḥammad hold it invalid because the Muslim himself cannot own wine or pork directly, so real equality is absent; the partnership then defaults to ʿInān (limited partnership). Finally, Mufāwaḍah must be explicitly stated using the term 'Mufāwaḍah' or by detailing all its conditions, because the average person is unfamiliar with its requirements. The text provides a sample wording for the contract.

Key Terms
شركة الملك (shirkat al-milk): Co-ownership of a specific asset arising from inheritance, purchase, gift, or mixing without a formal contract.
شركة العقد (shirkat al-'aqd): Contractual partnership where two or more agree to jointly engage in trade with shared capital and profits, based on offer and acceptance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
For Shirkat al-Milk (co-ownership), if one partner sells the jointly owned item to a third party without the other partner's consent, what is the ruling?
A) The sale is void because each partner is a stranger to the other's share.
B) The sale is valid if the item is not thoroughly mixed.
C) The sale is valid only if the buyer is a Muslim.
D) The sale is valid but the other partner can cancel it.

Key Takeaway
The key difference between co-ownership and contractual partnership is the presence or absence of agency, which determines the authority to dispose of the shared property.

Shirkah — Partnership
advanced
12 min
Mufāwaḍah: Conditions for Universal Partnership and the Muslim-Non-Muslim Dispute
Conditions of Mufāwaḍah and the dispute on Muslim-non-Muslim partnership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions required for a Mufāwaḍah partnership and evaluate the validity of such a partnership between a Muslim and a non-Muslim according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Mufāwaḍah requires full equality in capital, actions, and debt; otherwise it becomes 'Inan.

This lesson delves into the most comprehensive form of contractual partnership: Mufāwaḍah (universal partnership). The Hanafi school sets stringent conditions, including mutual agency and surety, and equality in capital, actions, and debt. A notable internal dispute arises regarding partnership between a Muslim and a dhimmī, with Abu Yusuf permitting it while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that it defaults to 'Inan. Understanding these conditions is essential for distinguishing between valid and invalid partnership structures.

Guided Path
1
Define Mufāwaḍah
Mufāwaḍah is a contractual partnership that includes both agency (wakāla) and surety (kafāla), with full equality between partners in capital, actions, and debt.
The term comes from tafwīḍ meaning equality.
2
Identify the three areas of equality
Partners must be equal in capital (amount and value), actions (authority to trade and manage), and debt (liability for each other's debts).
Equality must exist at the start and continue.
3
Recognize invalid partners
Mufāwaḍah is invalid between a free person and a slave, between an adult and a minor, and between a Muslim and a non-Muslim (according to the majority).
The impediment is lack of capacity or equality.
4
Analyze the Muslim-dhimmī dispute
Abu Yusuf allows it because the Muslim can own wine/pork via an agent. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say it is invalid because the Muslim cannot directly own those items, thus equality fails.
The practical result: if invalid, it becomes 'Inan partnership.
5
Apply the formal condition
Mufāwaḍah must be explicitly stated using the term or by detailing all conditions. A sample wording is provided in the text.
This avoids ambiguity since most people are unaware of the fine conditions.

The Book of Partnership: Rulings of Partnership. The Book of Partnership means explaining its rulings. Co-ownership: that two individuals own a specific property by inheritance or purchase, and each is a stranger regarding the share of his partner. Contractual partnership: that one of them says: 'I have partnered with you'... The Book of Partnership: [It is derived from 'sharaka' meaning he made him a partner in wealth, and 'sharik' and 'musharik' are the same. Legally: it is a contract of mixing two or more shares such that one share cannot be distinguished from the other. (Ayni, Ma'din)]... Partnership is proven by the Book (Quran): 'They are partners in the third' (4:12), and by his statement (peace be upon him): 'Allah says: I am the third of the two partners as long as one does not betray the other. If he betrays, I depart from them.' And by consensus of the imams on its permissibility. And by reason: it is a means of seeking bounty, which is legislated by His statement: 'that you seek with your wealth' (4:24). Linguistically, partnership means mixing of shares. Then legally this name is applied to the contract of partnership, as the contract is the cause of mixing. Thus the legal meaning is more specific than the linguistic. Its pillar in the contract is mixing. Its condition of permissibility: that the property be capable of partnership. Its ruling: that the combined property from the two shares becomes jointly owned between them. Co-ownership: partnership is of two types: first, co-ownership... (Ayni). The restriction to two is incidental, because the ruling for three or more does not differ. (Ma'din) 'By inheritance' meaning from an estate, 'or purchase' meaning by buying, or gift, or acquisition by conquest, or their wealth mixed, or they mixed it such that distinguishing becomes difficult or impossible – all this is co-ownership. His statement: 'each is a stranger regarding the share of his partner' because it does not include agency. In some copies 'regarding the share of another' – the first is better because 'another' includes cases where the other is not a partner, unlike 'partner' which specifies that he is his partner. Neither may dispose of it without the permission of his partner. If he sells his share to his partner, it is permissible in any case. Likewise if he sells it to someone else, except in the case of mixing (khalṭ and ikhtilāṭ); then he may not sell it to a stranger except with the consent of his partner. (Fath) Contractual partnership: the second type of partnership, contractual partnership. Its pillar is offer and acceptance, as indicated by his statement: 'one of them says' etc. Its condition is that it be in something that accepts agency, so that each in purchasing is an agent for himself regarding half and an agent for his partner regarding the other half; thus what is purchased becomes shared between them, and earnings likewise according to the partnership. Hence it is not valid in gathering firewood, grass, and similar common property, because agency is not valid in them. (Ramz al-Haqā'iq 1/464) (Mukhtaṣar)... The Book of Partnership: Rulings of Partnership. ... and the other accepts – it is Mufāwaḍah if it includes agency and surety, and they are equal in capital, actions, and debt. So it is not valid between a free person and a slave, meaning regarding actions... Mufāwaḍah partnership, from the two partners. The other accepts by saying 'I accept', because it is a contract, so offer and acceptance are necessary. It is Mufāwaḍah: The author, following the author of al-Hidāyah, made contractual partnership into four types: Mufāwaḍah, ʿInān, Taqabbul, and Wujūh. But there is a contention, because it implies that partnership of crafts (ṣanāʾiʿ) and credit (wujūh) are distinct from Mufāwaḍah and ʿInān, which is not the case. The better division is that of al-Ṭaḥāwī and others: contractual partnership is of three kinds: by capital, labor, and credit; each can be Mufāwaḍah or ʿInān. Mufāwaḍah is from tafwīḍ meaning equality in everything. This contract is named thus because equality is required in all respects. His statement: 'if it includes agency and surety' – as for the first, because partnership is realized as we explained. As for the second, because equality is realized, for Mufāwaḍah demands it. It is said 'fāwaḍa' meaning he made equal. So equality must be realized at the beginning and end, meaning each of the two partners is an agent for the other in trade operations and their adjuncts, and a guarantor for the other regarding trade liabilities and their appendages like hiring, transport, laundering, and dyeing. Thus each partner is liable due to the trade of the other. If you say: agency for an unknown thing is not permissible, so this partnership should be invalid? I say: agency for the unknown is not intended directly; it is implicit. Hence Muḍārabah is valid despite ignorance, because it is agency to buy something unknown within the contract. The same applies here. If you say: surety (kafālah) is not valid except with the acceptance of the guarantee in the session, so how is it valid here when the guarantee is unknown? I say: that is regarding surety intended directly; but if it enters as part of another contract, it is not required, as in agency. (Ayni, Fath) 'In capital' meaning regarding capital and profit. (Ayni) 'And debt' meaning regarding debt, because Mufāwaḍah indicates equality, and that is only with what is mentioned. (Ayni) So it is not valid between a free person and a slave, because a free adult has capacity for disposition and surety, whereas a slave has none of that except with the permission of his master. His statement: 'and a minor' – meaning Mufāwaḍah is not valid between a minor and an adult, because the minor cannot dispose except with the master's permission, and cannot provide surety even with permission. His statement: 'and a Muslim' and 'a non-Muslim' – meaning it is not valid between a Muslim and a non-Muslim according to the two (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad), but Abū Yūsuf allowed it despite difference of religion, e.g., if a Muslim and a dhimmī contract Mufāwaḍah, because what the dhimmī may own in buying wine and pork, the Muslim can own by appointing someone else as agent; thus equality is realized, but it is disliked. According to the two, it is not valid and the partnership becomes ʿInān, because the dhimmī owns that himself while the Muslim does not, so equality is missing. Furthermore, Mufāwaḍah is not valid except with the word 'Mufāwaḍah' or by explicit mention of all its requirements, because most people do not know its conditions; thus it must be explicitly stated or its implications mentioned so that it is clear and apparent. For example, one says to the other, both being free, adult, Muslim: 'I have partnered with you in all the cash I own, on the basis of general delegation from each of us to the other in trades, cash, and credit, on condition that each of us is guarantor for the other regarding whatever is necessary from any sale.' And in every place where Mufāwaḍah is not valid =
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Partnership (Kitāb al-Shirkah) in the Hanafi fiqh manual. It begins by defining partnership linguistically (mixing of shares) and legally (a contract of mixing shares that become indistinguishable). The author establishes the legitimacy of partnership from four sources: the Quran (4:12, where heirs are called partners), the Hadith Qudsi where Allah declares Himself the third partner as long as there is no betrayal, scholarly consensus (ijma), and rational reasoning (ma'qul) since partnership is a means of seeking lawful provision, which is encouraged in the Quran (4:24). The legal meaning is more specific than the linguistic because the contract is the cause of the mixing. The pillar (rukn) in a contractual partnership is the mixing itself, and its condition is that the property be capable of being subject to partnership. The ruling is that the combined property becomes jointly owned. The author then distinguishes two types: 'Shirkat al-Milk' (co-ownership) and 'Shirkat al-'Aqd' (contractual partnership). In co-ownership, two or more individuals come to own a specific asset through inheritance, purchase, gift, conquest, or accidental mixing where shares cannot be distinguished. Importantly, neither partner can dispose of the asset (e.g., sell it to a third party) without the consent of the other, unless selling to the partner himself. However, in cases where the goods have been thoroughly mixed (khalṭ or ikhtilāṭ), even selling to a third party requires mutual consent. This is because co-ownership does not inherently include agency; each partner is a stranger to the other's share. The second type is contractual partnership (Shirkat al-'Aqd), whose pillar is offer and acceptance. Its condition is that the subject matter must be something in which agency is valid, so that each partner acts as both principal (for his own half) and agent (for his partner's half). Thus they can jointly purchase and share profits. This type is not valid for gathering firewood or grass from open lands, as agency cannot be performed for such common properties. The passage then delves into the sub-type of Mufāwaḍah (universal partnership). Mufāwaḍah requires the inclusion of both agency (wakāla) and surety (kafāla) between the partners, and full equality in three areas: capital, actions (authority to trade and manage), and debt liabilities. The reason is that Mufāwaḍah literally means equality, and the contract is built upon it. The author addresses potential objections: agency for an unknown thing is normally invalid, but here it is implicit within the partnership contract—similar to the validity of Muḍārabah despite ignorance of the specific purchase. Similarly, surety normally requires explicit acceptance of the beneficiary in the session, but when it is part of a larger contract, it is valid by implication. Mufāwaḍah is invalid between a free person and a slave because a slave lacks capacity for independent disposition and surety, even with the master's permission. It is also invalid between a minor and an adult for the same reason. A notable intra-Hanafi dispute is presented: Abū Yūsuf allowed Mufāwaḍah between a Muslim and a non-Muslim dhimmī, arguing that the Muslim can own wine and pork through an agent, thus achieving legal equality, though he considered it makruh (disliked). However, the two (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad, or in some texts Abū Yūsuf's own later view?) According to the preferred Hanafi position, Abū Ḥanīfa and Muḥammad hold it invalid because the Muslim himself cannot own wine or pork directly, so real equality is absent; the partnership then defaults to ʿInān (limited partnership). Finally, Mufāwaḍah must be explicitly stated using the term 'Mufāwaḍah' or by detailing all its conditions, because the average person is unfamiliar with its requirements. The text provides a sample wording for the contract.

Key Terms
مفاوضة (mufāwaḍah): Universal partnership where partners are equal in capital, actions, and debt, and each is agent and guarantor for the other.
عنان (ʿinān): Limited partnership where partners contribute capital but are not necessarily equal and do not provide mutual surety.
وكالة (wakālah): Agency; one partner acts as agent for the other in trade.
كفالة (kafālah): Surety; each partner is guarantor for the other's debts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a condition for a valid Mufāwaḍah according to the Hanafi school?
A) Partners must be equal in capital.
B) Partners must be equal in actions.
C) Partners must be equal in debt liability.
D) Partners must be from the same profession.

Key Takeaway
Mufāwaḍah is a strict universal partnership requiring full equality; failure to meet its conditions converts it to 'Inan, affecting profit distribution.

Definition and Conditions of Shirkat al-Mufawada
Defines Mufawada partnership and its essential elements of agency and surety

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of Shirkat al-Mufawada and list its required conditions according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Mufawada requires complete equality and mutual agency/suretyship, or else it is not valid.

This lesson introduces Shirkat al-Mufawada, a unique partnership contract in Hanafi law. The passage defines its essential elements: mutual agency (wakala) and mutual suretyship (kafala), along with strict equality between partners in capital, authority, and debt liability. Understanding these conditions is crucial to distinguish Mufawada from other partnership types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract and its elements
Recognize that Shirkat al-Mufawada is defined by the inclusion of both agency and suretyship, and equality in mal, tasarruf, and dayn.
2
Analyze the three equality requirements
Explain why equality in capital, authority, and debt liability is essential for the partnership to be a true Mufawada.
3
Examine the agency and suretyship implications
Understand that each partner acts as agent and surety for the other, creating mutual liability.
4
Address the objections
The commentary raises two objections: agency for unknown and suretyship without acceptance. Learn the Hanafi responses based on implicit authorization.
5
Summarize the conditions
List all shurut for a valid Mufawada contract: offer and acceptance, inclusion of agency and suretyship, and equality in mal, tasarruf, and dayn.

Commentary
This passage defines Shirkat al-Mufawada, one of the types of partnership contracts in Hanafi law. The matn states that Mufawada requires two elements: (1) the contract including both mutual agency (wakala) and mutual suretyship (kafala), and (2) complete equality between the partners in three respects: the capital they contribute (mal), their authority to transact (tasarruf), and their liability for debts (dayn). The commentary explains that offer and acceptance are necessary for validity. It also addresses the objection that agency for an unknown matter is invalid, but the Hanafi response is that such agency is included implicitly in a broader contract, similar to mudarabah. Similarly, suretyship without acceptance of the creditor is normally invalid, but when it enters as a necessary incident of the partnership, it is permissible. This ruling is based on the custom of the people and the principle that the contract of Mufawada is recognized in Islamic law.

Key Terms
مفاوضة (mufāwaḍah): A type of partnership where partners are equal in capital, authority, and liability, and each is both agent and surety for the other.
وكالة (wakālah): Agency; each partner acts as the agent of the other in trade.
كفالة (kafālah): Suretyship; each partner is surety for the other regarding trade-related debts.

Key Takeaway
Shirkat al-Mufawada is a contract of complete equality requiring mutual agency and suretyship, valid only when conditions of equality in capital, authority, and debt are met.

Invalidity of Mufawada Between Unequal Partners
Explains why Mufawada is invalid between unequal partners

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three cases where Mufawada is invalid and explain why it defaults to an 'inan partnership, citing the Hanafi reliance on istihsan.

Core Idea
Mufawada requires perfect equality; if absent, the contract becomes a valid 'inan partnership.

In this lesson, we examine a key restriction on Shirkat al-Mufawada: the partners must be equal in capacity for agency and suretyship. The matn lists three prohibited pairings—free with slave, adult with minor, Muslim with non-Muslim—and the commentary explains the underlying rationale. We also see a striking intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the Muslim-non-Muslim case and a cross-madhhab dispute with al-Shafi'i and Malik over the very validity of Mufawada. Understanding these boundaries is essential to correctly structuring partnerships in Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three prohibited pairings
Locate in the source text the three categories: free/slave, adult/minor, Muslim/non-Muslim. Note the reasoning given for each: the slave lacks independent authority, the minor lacks it, and the non-Muslim can deal in forbidden items.
The text explicitly says 'fa la tasihh' for each.
2
Analyze the equality condition
The core condition for Mufawada is equality (musawat) in the capacity to transact and guarantee. Explain why each pairing violates this condition.
For Muslim/non-Muslim, the inequality arises because the non-Muslim can lawfully buy wine and pork, which the Muslim cannot.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that Mufawada between Muslim and non-Muslim is invalid and becomes 'inan. Abu Yusuf permits it as valid but makruh. Understand each position's basis.
Abu Yusuf argues that the Muslim can appoint an agent to buy prohibited items, thus achieving equality.
4
Understand the cross-madhhab dispute
Al-Shafi'i and Malik reject Mufawada entirely due to unknown agency and suretyship. The Hanafi school responds with istihsan based on custom and the hadith 'Fawwidu'.
The Hanafi response overrides qiyas (analogy) with istihsan because of established practice.
5
Apply the default ruling
When Mufawada is invalid, the contract automatically becomes a valid 'inan partnership. This is because the conditions for 'inan (known capital, no mutual agency/suretyship) are still met.
This preserves the parties' intent to form a partnership while respecting the limits of Sharia.

(عيني، مستخلص، فتح) وصبي: أي ولا بين صبي وبالغ، ومثل الصبي المجنون والمعتوه. (فتح)

Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage discusses the invalidity of Shirkat al-Mufawada between partners who are not equal in capacity for agency and suretyship. The matn lists three pairs: free person and slave, adult and minor, Muslim and non-Muslim. The commentary explains that a slave lacks the authority to transact or guarantee without his master's permission; a minor similarly lacks such capacity; and a non-Muslim (dhimmi) can lawfully trade in items like wine and pork, which a Muslim cannot, so complete equality is impossible. Abu Yusuf differs from Abu Hanifa and Muhammad on the Muslim-non-Muslim case: he considers the partnership valid but disliked because the Muslim can appoint an agent to buy those items. The two (Sahibayn) hold that it is not valid as Mufawada and defaults to 'inan. The passage then presents the Shafi'i objection that Mufawada is invalid due to unknown agency and surety, but the Hanafi response relies on istihsan based on continuous custom and the hadith 'Fawwidu, for it is greater in blessing.' This illustrates a fundamental Hanafi usul principle: established custom ('urf) can override qiyas.

Key Terms
عنان (ʿinān): A type of partnership where partners share profits proportionally to their capital, but are not mutual agents or sureties. It is the default partnership when Mufawada conditions are not met.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference; a method of departing from qiyas (analogy) in favor of a ruling based on custom, need, or stronger evidence.
مفاوضة (mufāwaḍah): A type of partnership where partners are equal in capital, profit share, and legal capacity, and each is a mutual agent and surety for the other.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It is invalid as Mufawada and becomes 'inan
C) It is completely void and cannot be a partnership at all
D) It is valid but only with the word 'Mufawada' explicitly stated

Key Takeaway
Mufawada requires perfect equality; if not equal, the contract defaults to a valid 'inan partnership.

Joint Ownership of Purchases and the Necessity Exception
Details joint ownership of purchases and the necessities exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general rule that purchases by one Mufawada partner are jointly owned and identify the exceptions for personal necessities.

Core Idea
In Mufawada, each partner's purchase is joint — except for food, clothing, and other necessities that remain personal.

The passage begins by explaining the fundamental effect of a Mufawada contract: equality. Each partner stands in the place of the other in transactions, so a purchase by one is considered a purchase by both. However, there is a crucial exception: purchases for personal necessities like food, clothing, and housing are not joint, even if made with permission. This lesson unpacks that general rule and its exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
The text states that the requirement of Mufawada is equality, and each partner acts for the other. So any purchase by one is joint.
2
Note the condition of permission
The rule applies when the purchase is with the permission of the partner.
The author points to the end of the book of partnership for this condition.
3
Recognize the explicit exception
The text says 'except food for his family and their clothing.' This includes food, condiments, and clothing for self and family.
4
Expand the exception to other necessities
Commentary adds: hiring a house for residence, a mount for travel (e.g., hajj), and a slave-girl for intercourse or service.
These are all personal necessities (hawa'ij).
5
Understand the rationale
The exception is due to necessity (darurah); recurrent needs are known to occur and would cause hardship if shared.
This is a practical concession to avoid disruption of daily life.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يشتريه كل إلخ: لأن مقتضى العقد المساواة، وكل واحد منهما قائم مقام صاحبه في التصرف فكان شراء أحدهما كشرائهما، وهو محمول على ما إذا كان الشراء بإذن شريكه كما نبه عليه المصنف في آخر كتاب الشركة قوله إلا طعام أهله وكسوتهم"، أراد بالمستثنى ما كان من حوائجه، أي إذا اشترى من الطعام والإدام والكسوة لنفسه أو لأهله، لا يقع مشتركا ولو بإذن الشريك؛ لأن هذه الأشياء مستثناة عن المفاوضة للضرورة، فإن الحاجة الراتبة معلومة الوقوع، وكذا استيجار البيت لسكناه، أو دابة للركوب في حاجته كالحج وغيره، أو جارية للوطء أو الاستخدام. (مستخلص، فتح) وكل دين إلخ كثمن المبيع في البيع الجائز أو قيمته في الفاسد، وأجرة ما استأجره ولو لنفسه، ومهر المشتراة الموطوءة إذا استحقت، وما لزمه بالاستقراض فإنه يلزم الآخر تحقيقاً للمساواة، وإظهاراً لمعنى الكفالة؛ لأن أحدهما كفيل عن الآخر. وقوله : أو غصب، أي الدين الذي لزم أحدهما بسبب الغصب، وأراد به ما يشبه التجارة، فدخل ضمان الاستهلاك كالمستهلك من الوديعة، وغيرها، فيكون على الشريكين عند الطرفين، وقال أبو يوسف: يختص به الغاصب ولا يلزم لشريكه؛ لأنه ليس بتجارة، فصار كارش الجناية، ولهما: أنه ملك المضمون عند أداء الضمان فينقلب ضمان التجارة. وقوله: "أو كفالة" أى لو كفل أحدهما بمال عن أجنبي لزم الآخر عند أبي حنيفة ، وقالا: لا يلزمه؛ لأن الكفالة تبرع، فصار كالإقراض والكفالة بالنفس، ولأبي حنيفة: أنه تبرع ابتداء، ومعاوضة انتهاء؛ لأنه يستوجب على المكفول عنه بما أدى إذا كانت الكفالة بأمره، فبالنظر إلى البقاء يصح، وبالنظر إلى الابتداء لا يصح من الصبي والعبد المأذون، والمكاتب وأما الإقراض فهو على الخلاف أيضاً، ولو سلم فهو إعارة، فيكون لمثلها حكم عينها، لا حكم البدل، فلا معاوضة فيه، وأما الكفالة بالنفس فلا معاوضة فيها، فمطلق الكفالة محمول على المقيد، وهو ما إذا كانت الكفالة بأمره، وهو الصحيح. وقيد بقوله: "بتجارة وغصب وكفالة" احترازاً عن الدين الذي لزم أحدهما بسبب لا تصح فيه الشركة كالجنايات والنكاح والخلع والصلح عن دم عمد، ونفقة الزوجات والأقارب؛ لأن هذه الديون بدل عما لا يصح الاشتراك فيه، فلا تلزم إلا المباشر؛ لأن كل واحد منهما لم يلتزم بالعقد عن صاحبه إلا ديون التجارة (مستخلص، فتح، عيني) لا الضمان يصح، ولهذا
And 'what each buys, etc.' because the requirement of the contract is equality, and each of them stands in the place of his partner in transactions, so the purchase of one is like the purchase of both. This applies when the purchase is with the permission of his partner, as the author pointed out at the end of the book of partnership. His words 'except food for his family and their clothing': he meant by the exception what is from his necessities, i.e., if he buys food, condiments, and clothing for himself or his family, it does not become joint property even with the partner's permission, because these things are excepted from Mufawada due to necessity, since recurrent need is known to occur. Similarly, hiring a house for residence, or a mount for travel for need like hajj etc., or a slave girl for intercourse or service. (Mustakhlas, Fath) And 'every debt, etc.' like the price of a sold item in a valid sale or its value in an invalid one, and the rent of what he hired even for himself, and the dower of a purchased slave girl who is cohabited with if she is reclaimed, and what becomes obligatory by borrowing: it is binding on the other partner to realize equality and to manifest the meaning of suretyship, because each is surety for the other. And his words 'or ghasp' indicates the debt that becomes binding on one due to usurpation, and he meant by it what resembles trade, so it includes liability for consumption like consumed goods from deposit etc. So it is upon both partners according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), and Abu Yusuf said: it is specific to the usurper and is not binding on his partner because it is not trade, so it is like the bloodwit for injury. According to the two: he acquires the title to the guaranteed item upon paying the guarantee, so it transforms into trade guarantee. And his words 'or kafala' meaning if one gives a surety for property for a third party, it is binding on the other according to Abu Hanifa, and the two said: it is not binding on him because surety is a donation, so it is like lending and surety for the person. According to Abu Hanifa: it is a donation initially but a exchange ultimately, because he becomes entitled to claim from the person guaranteed for what he paid if the surety was by his order. So with regard to the ultimate result it is valid, and with regard to the beginning it is not valid from a child, a permitted slave, and a mukatib. As for lending, there is also a difference. Even if it is considered, it is a loan, so it has the ruling of the substance, not the substitute, so there is no exchange in it. As for surety for the person, there is no exchange in it. So the absolute surety is restricted to what is by his order, and that is the correct position. He restricted by his words 'by trade, ghasp, and kafala' to exclude debts incurred due to a cause in which partnership is not valid, like crimes, marriage, khul', settlement for intentional killing, and maintenance of wives and relatives; because these debts are compensation for what cannot be jointly owned, so they are only binding on the direct doer. Because each of them did not undertake by the contract for his partner except trade debts. (Mustakhlas, Fath, Ayni) The guarantee is not valid, and for this reason.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage details the effects of a valid Mufawada contract. First, it states that any purchase made by one partner is joint property, unless it is for personal necessities like food, clothing, housing, or transport for himself and his family. This exception is due to necessity, as these needs are regular and personal. Second, it discusses debts: all trade-related debts (sale price, rent, dower of a slave-girl, borrowed amounts) are joint because each partner is surety for the other. Then it covers two disputed points: (1) ghasp (usurpation): According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, a debt arising from ghasp is joint because upon payment, the usurper acquires ownership of the guaranteed item, thus it becomes like a trade debt. Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing ghasp is not trade. (2) kafala (suretyship): If one partner gives a surety for a debt of a third party, it is joint according to Abu Hanifa (because ultimately it becomes an exchange), but the two (Sahibayn) say it is not joint because surety is a donation. The correct opinion (al-sahih) is that the surety is restricted to where it was by the debtor's order. Finally, the passage lists debts that are not joint: those arising from crimes, marriage, khul', settlement for intentional murder, and family maintenance, because these are not trade-related.

Key Terms
مفاوضة (mufāwaḍah): A type of partnership in Hanafi law where partners have equal capital, profit, and authority, and each is a surety for the other.
ضرورة (ḍarūrah): Necessity; recurrent personal needs that exempt purchases from joint ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following purchases by one Mufawada partner becomes joint property?
A) Buying food for his family's dinner
B) Renting a house for his personal residence
C) Purchasing trade inventory with the partner's permission
D) Buying a slave-girl for personal service

Key Takeaway
In Mufawada, the default is joint ownership of purchases, but personal necessities are excepted to prevent hardship.

Shirkah — Partnership

Joint Liability for Trade Debts in Mufawada
Lists trade debts that are jointly binding on both partners

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why trade debts (sale price, rent, borrowed money, etc.) are jointly binding on both Mufawada partners.

Core Idea
Each partner is surety for the other, so all trade-related debts are joint to realize equality and mutual guarantee.

The passage continues by discussing debts. The general rule is that any debt arising from trade transactions (valid or invalid sales, hiring, borrowing, etc.) is binding on both partners. This is because the Mufawada contract implies that each partner is a surety (kafil) for the other. The text provides examples and emphasizes that this applies even if the debt was incurred by only one partner for his own benefit (e.g., renting something for himself). This lesson focuses on the rule and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category of debts
The text says 'every debt' from trade (thaman al-mabi' in valid sale, qimah in invalid sale, ujrah for hire, mahr of a slave-girl if reclaimed, qard borrowed).
These are all debts that arise from typical commercial activities.
2
State the ruling
Such debts are binding on the other partner (yazim al-akhar).
The other partner must contribute to payment.
3
Understand the rationale
Two reasons are given: (1) to achieve equality (tahqiqan li al-musawah), (2) to manifest the meaning of suretyship (kaffalah) since each is a surety for the other.
4
Note that the debt is joint even if for personal use
The text says 'even if he hired for himself' (wa-law li nafsihi).
This emphasizes the strength of the guarantee; it doesn't matter if the benefit was personal.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafālah): Suretyship; each Mufawada partner is a surety for the other's trade-related debts.
مهر (mahr): Dower; the payment given by a husband to his wife upon marriage, but here it refers to the dower of a purchased slave-girl.

Disputed Debts: Ghasp and Kafala in Mufawada
Presents intra-Hanafi disputes on ghasp and kafala debts with proofs and tarjih

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi disputes regarding joint liability for debts from ghasp (usurpation) and kafala (suretyship) and identify the mufta bihi positions.

Core Idea
Ghasp and kafala debts are joint per Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but Abu Yusuf and the Sahibayn dissent on different grounds; the correct position restricts kafala to cases by the debtor's order.

The passage now addresses two specific types of debts that are controversial even within the Hanafi school: debts arising from ghasp (wrongful taking) and kafala (suretyship for a third party). The text presents the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani with their respective proofs. It also clarifies the preferred view (al-sahih) for kafala. This lesson analyses these disputes and their practical outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first dispute: ghasp
The text says 'or ghasp' — a debt from usurpation. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, it is joint. According to Abu Yusuf, it is not.
Abu Yusuf argues it is not trade; the two argue that upon paying, the guarantor acquires ownership, converting it into a trade guarantee.
2
Understand the reasoning for ghasp
The two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) argue: when the usurper pays the value of the usurped item, he effectively buys it, thus it becomes a trade debt. Abu Yusuf says it is a tort (jinaayah) akin to bloodwit.
The text uses qiyas: ghasp is compared to trade because ownership transfers upon payment.
3
Identify the second dispute: kafala
If one partner gives a surety for a third party's debt, Abu Hanifa says it is joint; the two (Sahibayn) say it is not.
Sahibayn argue surety is a donation (tabarru'), like lending.
4
Understand Abu Hanifa's proof for kafala
Abu Hanifa argues that suretyship is a donation initially but becomes an exchange ultimately (if the surety pays, he can claim from the debtor). So with regard to the outcome, it is valid.
The text explicitly says 'it is a donation initially, an exchange ultimately.'
5
Identify the mufta bihi for kafala
The text states that the correct position (al-sahih) is that kafala is joint only when it is by the order of the debtor (bi amrihi). This restricts Abu Hanifa's absolute view.
The phrase 'al-sahih' indicates the relied-upon ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يشتريه كل إلخ: لأن مقتضى العقد المساواة، وكل واحد منهما قائم مقام صاحبه في التصرف فكان شراء أحدهما كشرائهما، وهو محمول على ما إذا كان الشراء بإذن شريكه كما نبه عليه المصنف في آخر كتاب الشركة قوله إلا طعام أهله وكسوتهم"، أراد بالمستثنى ما كان من حوائجه، أي إذا اشترى من الطعام والإدام والكسوة لنفسه أو لأهله، لا يقع مشتركا ولو بإذن الشريك؛ لأن هذه الأشياء مستثناة عن المفاوضة للضرورة، فإن الحاجة الراتبة معلومة الوقوع، وكذا استيجار البيت لسكناه، أو دابة للركوب في حاجته كالحج وغيره، أو جارية للوطء أو الاستخدام. (مستخلص، فتح) وكل دين إلخ كثمن المبيع في البيع الجائز أو قيمته في الفاسد، وأجرة ما استأجره ولو لنفسه، ومهر المشتراة الموطوءة إذا استحقت، وما لزمه بالاستقراض فإنه يلزم الآخر تحقيقاً للمساواة، وإظهاراً لمعنى الكفالة؛ لأن أحدهما كفيل عن الآخر. وقوله : أو غصب، أي الدين الذي لزم أحدهما بسبب الغصب، وأراد به ما يشبه التجارة، فدخل ضمان الاستهلاك كالمستهلك من الوديعة، وغيرها، فيكون على الشريكين عند الطرفين، وقال أبو يوسف: يختص به الغاصب ولا يلزم لشريكه؛ لأنه ليس بتجارة، فصار كارش الجناية، ولهما: أنه ملك المضمون عند أداء الضمان فينقلب ضمان التجارة. وقوله: "أو كفالة" أى لو كفل أحدهما بمال عن أجنبي لزم الآخر عند أبي حنيفة ، وقالا: لا يلزمه؛ لأن الكفالة تبرع، فصار كالإقراض والكفالة بالنفس، ولأبي حنيفة: أنه تبرع ابتداء، ومعاوضة انتهاء؛ لأنه يستوجب على المكفول عنه بما أدى إذا كانت الكفالة بأمره، فبالنظر إلى البقاء يصح، وبالنظر إلى الابتداء لا يصح من الصبي والعبد المأذون، والمكاتب وأما الإقراض فهو على الخلاف أيضاً، ولو سلم فهو إعارة، فيكون لمثلها حكم عينها، لا حكم البدل، فلا معاوضة فيه، وأما الكفالة بالنفس فلا معاوضة فيها، فمطلق الكفالة محمول على المقيد، وهو ما إذا كانت الكفالة بأمره، وهو الصحيح. وقيد بقوله: "بتجارة وغصب وكفالة" احترازاً عن الدين الذي لزم أحدهما بسبب لا تصح فيه الشركة كالجنايات والنكاح والخلع والصلح عن دم عمد، ونفقة الزوجات والأقارب؛ لأن هذه الديون بدل عما لا يصح الاشتراك فيه، فلا تلزم إلا المباشر؛ لأن كل واحد منهما لم يلتزم بالعقد عن صاحبه إلا ديون التجارة (مستخلص، فتح، عيني) لا الضمان يصح، ولهذا
And 'what each buys, etc.' because the requirement of the contract is equality, and each of them stands in the place of his partner in transactions, so the purchase of one is like the purchase of both. This applies when the purchase is with the permission of his partner, as the author pointed out at the end of the book of partnership. His words 'except food for his family and their clothing': he meant by the exception what is from his necessities, i.e., if he buys food, condiments, and clothing for himself or his family, it does not become joint property even with the partner's permission, because these things are excepted from Mufawada due to necessity, since recurrent need is known to occur. Similarly, hiring a house for residence, or a mount for travel for need like hajj etc., or a slave girl for intercourse or service. (Mustakhlas, Fath) And 'every debt, etc.' like the price of a sold item in a valid sale or its value in an invalid one, and the rent of what he hired even for himself, and the dower of a purchased slave girl who is cohabited with if she is reclaimed, and what becomes obligatory by borrowing: it is binding on the other partner to realize equality and to manifest the meaning of suretyship, because each is surety for the other. And his words 'or ghasp' indicates the debt that becomes binding on one due to usurpation, and he meant by it what resembles trade, so it includes liability for consumption like consumed goods from deposit etc. So it is upon both partners according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), and Abu Yusuf said: it is specific to the usurper and is not binding on his partner because it is not trade, so it is like the bloodwit for injury. According to the two: he acquires the title to the guaranteed item upon paying the guarantee, so it transforms into trade guarantee. And his words 'or kafala' meaning if one gives a surety for property for a third party, it is binding on the other according to Abu Hanifa, and the two said: it is not binding on him because surety is a donation, so it is like lending and surety for the person. According to Abu Hanifa: it is a donation initially but a exchange ultimately, because he becomes entitled to claim from the person guaranteed for what he paid if the surety was by his order. So with regard to the ultimate result it is valid, and with regard to the beginning it is not valid from a child, a permitted slave, and a mukatib. As for lending, there is also a difference. Even if it is considered, it is a loan, so it has the ruling of the substance, not the substitute, so there is no exchange in it. As for surety for the person, there is no exchange in it. So the absolute surety is restricted to what is by his order, and that is the correct position. He restricted by his words 'by trade, ghasp, and kafala' to exclude debts incurred due to a cause in which partnership is not valid, like crimes, marriage, khul', settlement for intentional killing, and maintenance of wives and relatives; because these debts are compensation for what cannot be jointly owned, so they are only binding on the direct doer. Because each of them did not undertake by the contract for his partner except trade debts. (Mustakhlas, Fath, Ayni) The guarantee is not valid, and for this reason.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage details the effects of a valid Mufawada contract. First, it states that any purchase made by one partner is joint property, unless it is for personal necessities like food, clothing, housing, or transport for himself and his family. This exception is due to necessity, as these needs are regular and personal. Second, it discusses debts: all trade-related debts (sale price, rent, dower of a slave-girl, borrowed amounts) are joint because each partner is surety for the other. Then it covers two disputed points: (1) ghasp (usurpation): According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, a debt arising from ghasp is joint because upon payment, the usurper acquires ownership of the guaranteed item, thus it becomes like a trade debt. Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing ghasp is not trade. (2) kafala (suretyship): If one partner gives a surety for a debt of a third party, it is joint according to Abu Hanifa (because ultimately it becomes an exchange), but the two (Sahibayn) say it is not joint because surety is a donation. The correct opinion (al-sahih) is that the surety is restricted to where it was by the debtor's order. Finally, the passage lists debts that are not joint: those arising from crimes, marriage, khul', settlement for intentional murder, and family maintenance, because these are not trade-related.

Key Terms
غصب (ghaṣb): Usurpation; wrongful taking of property, leading to liability for its value.
كفالة (kafālah): Suretyship; guaranteeing a debt of a third party.
تبرع (tabarru‘): Donation; a gratuitous act. The Sahibayn consider kafala pure tabarru'.
معاوضة (mu‘āwaḍah): Exchange; a transactional act. Abu Hanifa considers kafala ultimately an exchange.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) ruling regarding a debt arising from kafala in Mufawada?
A) It is always joint, per Abu Hanifa.
B) It is never joint, per the Sahibayn.
C) It is joint only if the surety was given by the debtor's order.
D) It is joint only if the debt is from trade.

Key Takeaway
Debts from ghasp are joint per the relied-upon view, while kafala is joint only if by the debtor's order.

Joint Ownership in Mufawada: The Household Necessity Exception
Ruling on joint ownership of purchases in mufawada, excluding food and clothing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default ruling that purchases in mufawada are jointly owned and identify the exception for household necessities, explaining the rationale of necessity.

Core Idea
In mufawada, all purchases are joint except personal household necessities—excluded by necessity.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq establishes a key rule in mufawada partnership: whatever each partner purchases (with permission) becomes jointly owned, except for food and clothing for the buyer's household. The commentary explains that this exception is based on necessity and also extends to other recurrent personal needs like housing and transport. Understanding this exception prevents disputes over personal versus business purchases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Default Ruling
Read the matn: 'whatever each purchases becomes shared.' This is the general rule for all purchases in a valid mufawada partnership.
The default applies only if the purchase is made with the partner's permission, as noted in the commentary.
2
Spot the Exception
The matn continues: 'except the food of his household and their clothing.' These personal necessities are excluded from joint ownership.
The exception is not limited to food and clothing; the commentary extends it to other recurring personal needs.
3
Understand the Rationale
The commentary states: 'because these things are excepted from mufawada due to necessity, for recurrent need is known to occur.' The reason is the practical necessity of daily living.
The equality condition of the contract would otherwise make all purchases joint, but necessity overrides.
4
Apply the Extension
The commentary adds analogous cases: renting a house for dwelling, hiring a riding animal for Hajj, or buying a slave-girl for service. These are also personal necessities.
This is a form of qiyas: the 'illa (legal cause) is recurrent personal necessity.
5
Confirm the Condition for Default
The commentary emphasizes that the default joint ownership applies only when the purchase is 'by the permission of the partner.' Without permission, the purchase is not automatically joint.
This condition is crucial for applying the ruling correctly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصبي وبالغ، ومسلم وكافر وما يشتريه كل يقع مشتركاً إلا طعام أهله وكسوتهم، لعدم المساواة بينهما. وصبي: أي ولا بين صبي وبالغ، ومثل الصبي المجنون والمعتوه. (فتح) وما يشتريه كل إلخ: لأن مقتضى العقد المساواة، وكل واحد منهما قائم مقام صاحبه في التصرف فكان شراء أحدهما كشرائهما، وهو محمول على ما إذا كان الشراء بإذن شريكه كما نبه عليه المصنف في آخر كتاب الشركة قوله إلا طعام أهله وكسوتهم، أراد بالمستثنى ما كان من حوائجه، أي إذا اشترى من الطعام والإدام والكسوة لنفسه أو لأهله، لا يقع مشتركا ولو بإذن الشريك؛ لأن هذه الأشياء مستثناة عن المفاوضة للضرورة، فإن الحاجة الراتبة معلومة الوقوع، وكذا استيجار البيت لسكناه، أو دابة للركوب في حاجته كالحج وغيره، أو جارية للوطء أو الاستخدام. (مستخلص، فتح)
And a child and an adult, and a Muslim and a disbeliever, and whatever each purchases becomes shared except the food of his household and their clothing, due to lack of equality between them. Comment: 'wa sabiyyun' meaning not between a child and an adult, and similarly the insane and mentally deficient. (Fath) 'And whatever each purchases etc.': because the requirement of the contract is equality, and each of them stands in the place of the partner in transactions, so the purchase of one is like the purchase of both, and it is interpreted as being when the purchase is by the permission of the partner as the author pointed out at the end of the Book of Partnership. His statement 'except the food of his household and their clothing': he intended by the exception what is from his needs, i.e., if he purchases food, oil, and clothing for himself or his household, it does not become joint even if with the permission of the partner, because these things are excepted from mufawada due to necessity, for recurrent need is known to occur, and likewise renting a house for his dwelling, or an animal for riding in his need like Hajj and other things, or a slave-girl for intercourse or service. (Mukhtalish, Fath)
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling of purchases in a mufawada partnership. The matn states that if a partnership is established between a child and an adult, or a Muslim and a non-Muslim, whatever each purchases becomes jointly owned between them, except for the food and clothing of the buyer's household. The reason given is 'lack of equality' in these items. The commentary clarifies that the exception includes the insane and mentally deficient, and emphasizes that the purchase is considered joint only if made with the partner's permission. The exception for household necessities is due to necessity, as these are recurrent needs. Similarly, renting a house for dwelling or a riding animal for travel, or purchasing a slave-girl for intercourse or service, are also excluded from joint ownership. This ruling is based on the principle of equality in mufawada, which is the requirement of the contract.

Key Terms
مفاوضة (mufāwaḍah): A partnership where each partner is both an agent and guarantor for the other, with full equality in capital and authority.
شريك (sharīk): One of the partners in mufawada; each stands in the place of the other in transactions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a valid mufawada partnership, which of the following purchases by one partner remains the exclusive property of that partner?
A) A computer for the shared office
B) Groceries for his household
C) A shipment of goods for resale
D) Raw materials for the partnership business

Key Takeaway
In mufawada, purchases are joint except for personal and household necessities like food and clothing, based on the principle of necessity overriding the equality requirement.

Debts Binding Both Partners in Mufawada: Types and Disagreements
Ruling on debts in mufawada with intra-Hanafi disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which debts incurred by one mufawada partner bind the other, explain the Hanafi majority position on usurpation and guarantee, and apply the ruling to new scenarios.

Core Idea
In mufawada, debts from trade bind both; for usurpation and guarantee, the Madhhab's majority holds they bind, but Abu Yusuf dissents.

The mufawada partnership is one of full agency and mutual guarantee. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies the extent of that guarantee: when one partner incurs a debt, does the other become liable? The matn gives a broad rule, but the commentary reveals important nuances and intra-Hanafi disagreements over usurpation and guarantee.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
The matn says any debt from trade, usurpation, or guarantee binds both. Understand that this is the baseline.
This rule applies to all debts arising from these three causes.
2
Examine the examples of trade debts
The commentary lists sale price, hire, dower, borrowing. These are all commercial transactions, so they clearly bind both.
Even if the partner rents something for his own use, the hire binds both.
3
Analyze the dispute on usurpation (ghasp)
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad consider ghasp akin to trade because it leads to ownership upon payment; Abu Yusuf treats it as a tort. Understand the reasoning and the thamarah.
The thamarah: in the majority view, both partners liable; in Abu Yusuf's view, only the usurper.
4
Analyze the dispute on guarantee (kafalah)
Abu Hanifa conditions it on being by the debtor's order; the Sahibayn see it as a pure donation. Understand the rationale.
The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is Abu Hanifa's when the guarantee is by order.
5
Identify the mufta bihi and exceptions
For ghasp, the majority view is relied upon. For kafalah, Abu Hanifa's view when by order. Also note excluded debts: crimes, marriage, etc.
These exclusions are because partnership in such matters is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكل دين لزم أحدهما بتجارة أو غصب أو كفالة لزم الآخر. وكل دين إلخ كثمن المبيع في البيع الجائز أو قيمته في الفاسد، وأجرة ما استأجره ولو لنفسه، ومهر المشتراة الموطوءة إذا استحقت، وما لزمه بالاستقراض فإنه يلزم الآخر تحقيقاً للمساواة، وإظهاراً لمعنى الكفالة؛ لأن أحدهما كفيل عن الآخر. وقوله : أو غصب، أي الدين الذي لزم أحدهما بسبب الغصب، وأراد به ما يشبه التجارة، فدخل ضمان الاستهلاك كالمستهلك من الوديعة ،وغيرها، فيكون على الشريكين عند الطرفين، وقال أبو يوسف: يختص به الغاصب ولا يلزم لشريكه؛ لأنه ليس بتجارة، فصار كارش الجناية، ولهما: أنه ملك المضمون عند أداء الضمان فينقلب ضمان التجارة. وقوله: 'أو كفالة' أى لو كفل أحدهما بمال عن أجنبي لزم الآخر عند أبي حنيفة ، وقالا: لا يلزمه؛ لأن الكفالة تبرع، فصار كالإقراض والكفالة بالنفس، ولأبي حنيفة: أنه تبرع ابتداء، ومعاوضة انتهاء؛ لأنه يستوجب على المكفول عنه بما أدى إذا كانت الكفالة بأمره، فبالنظر إلى البقاء يصح، وبالنظر إلى الابتداء لا يصح من الصبي والعبد المأذون ،والمكاتب وأما الإقراض فهو على الخلاف أيضاً، ولو سلم فهو إعارة، فيكون لمثلها حكم عينها، لا حكم البدل، فلا معاوضة فيه، وأما الكفالة بالنفس فلا معاوضة فيها، فمطلق الكفالة محمول على المقيد، وهو ما إذا كانت الكفالة بأمره، وهو الصحيح. وقيد بقوله: 'بتجارة وغصب وكفالة' احترازاً عن الدين الذي لزم أحدهما بسبب لا تصح فيه الشركة كالجنايات والنكاح والخلع والصلح عن دم عمد، ونفقة الزوجات والأقارب؛ لأن هذه الديون بدل عما لا يصح الاشتراك فيه، فلا تلزم إلا المباشر؛ لأن كل واحد منهما لم يلتزم بالعقد عن صاحبه إلا ديون التجارة (مستخلص، فتح، عيني)
And every debt incurred by one of them from trade, usurpation, or guarantee, becomes binding on the other. 'And every debt etc.': such as the price of goods in a valid sale or its value in an invalid one, the hire of what he rented even if for himself, the dower of a purchased slave-girl if she is entitled to it, and what becomes due by borrowing, for that becomes binding on the other to achieve equality and to manifest the meaning of guarantee, because one is the guarantor for the other. And his statement: 'or usurpation', i.e., the debt incurred by one due to usurpation, and he intended by it what resembles trade, so it includes guarantee of consumption like the consumed item from a deposit and others, so it is upon both partners according to the two masters (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And Abu Yusuf said: it is specific to the usurper and does not bind his partner, because it is not trade, so it becomes like the penalty for injury. And to them (the two masters): that the usurper owns the guaranteed thing when paying the guarantee, so it transforms into a trade guarantee. And his statement: 'or guarantee', meaning if one guarantees a debt for a stranger, it binds the other according to Abu Hanifa, but they (the two masters) said: it does not bind him, because guarantee is a voluntary donation, so it is like lending and personal guarantee. According to Abu Hanifa: it is a voluntary donation initially but a compensation finally, because he becomes entitled to what he paid from the guaranteed person if the guarantee was by his order, so from the perspective of continuation it is valid, and from the perspective of initiation it is not valid from a child, a permitted slave, or a mukatab. As for lending, it is also subject to disagreement; and if it is conceded, it is a loan for use, so it has the ruling of the specific item, not the substitute, so there is no compensation in it. As for personal guarantee, there is no compensation in it. So the absolute guarantee is interpreted as restricted, and that is when the guarantee is by his order, and that is the correct view. And he restricted it by his statement: 'from trade, usurpation, and guarantee' to exclude the debt incurred by one due to a cause in which partnership is not valid, like crimes, marriage, khul', and settlement for intentional blood, and maintenance of wives and relatives, because these debts are compensation for things in which partnership is not valid, so they only bind the direct party; because each of them did not undertake by contract for his partner except trade debts. (Mukhtalish, Fath, Ayni)
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on debts in mufawada partnership. The matn states that any debt incurred by one partner from trade, usurpation, or guarantee becomes binding on the other. The commentary then elaborates on each category. It clarifies that 'trade' includes sale price, hire, dower, and borrowed amounts. For usurpation (ghasp), Abu Hanifa and Muhammad consider it like trade and thus binding on both, but Abu Yusuf argues it is purely tortious and binds only the usurper. For guarantee (kafalah), Abu Hanifa sees it as ultimately compensatory if done by the debtor's order, so it binds both; the two masters say it is a donation and not binding. The passage also excludes debts from crimes, marriage, etc., as these are not partnership matters. The relied-upon position is that the guarantee is binding only if by the debtor's order.

Key Terms
غصب (ghaṣb): Usurpation of property; a debt arising from wrongful taking. In this context, it is considered akin to trade for partnership liability.
كفالة (kafālah): Guarantee of a debt; when done with the debtor's order, it becomes binding on both partners according to Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, which of the following debts incurred by one mufawada partner binds the other?
A) A diyah (blood money) for manslaughter
B) A borrowed sum used for business
C) A debt from usurpation of property
D) A dower for his wife

Key Takeaway
In mufawada, the mutual guarantee covers all trade-related debts and, by majority, also usurpation and guarantees by order, but not personal torts or family obligations.
