When Mufawada Becomes Invalid: Gifts and Inheritance
Ruling on when mufawada invalidates due to property receipt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a mufawada partnership is invalidated by a partner's receipt of property and distinguish between property types that do and do not affect the partnership.

Core Idea
Mufawada invalidates only when a partner receives property suitable as capital, not for goods or real estate.

In this lesson, we examine a key ruling from the Hanafi school on the mufawada partnership. While mufawada requires equality in capital, not every acquisition by a partner disrupts that equality. The commentator expands on the base text to clarify exactly when a gift or inheritance causes the mufawada to become invalid and convert to an 'inan partnership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the triggering event
Determine whether one partner has received a gift, inheritance, or (in some readings) borrowed funds. The ruling applies specifically to unilateral acquisition by one partner.
The text mentions 'gift or inheritance' as the primary triggers.
2
Classify the property type
Check if the received property is of a type in which partnership is valid (capital-suitable: gold, silver, bullion, current flus) or not (goods, real estate, debts before collection).
Only capital-suitable property affects equality; goods and real estate do not.
3
Apply the equality condition
If the property is capital-suitable, equality in capital is lost, so the mufawada becomes invalid. Otherwise, it remains valid.
The condition of equality is fundamental to mufawada.
4
Note the conversion to 'inan
When mufawada invalidates, it does not dissolve the partnership entirely but converts it to an 'inan partnership, which does not require equality.
The conversion is automatic due to the possibility of continuing partnership.
5
Consider the special case of debt inheritance
If one partner inherits a debt (even in dirhams or dinars), the mufawada does not become invalid until the debt is actually collected.
This is because the property is not yet in hand.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتبطل إن وهب لأحدهما، أو ورث ما أو استقراض شركة المفاوضة وتصير عنانا. أي إذا وهب أحد الشريكين أو ورث أو وصل إلى يده ما تصح فيه الشركة كالنقدين والتبر والفلس النافقين بطلت المفاوضة ، وصارت شركة عنان؛ لفوات المقصود، وهو المساواة في المال إلا أنه ينقلب عناناً للإمكان، فإن فيه المساواة ليس بشرط. (مستخلص،فتح) إن وهب لأحدهما : أي أحد المتفاوضين وقبل وقبض. (ط) تصح فيه الشركة: وهو النقدان لفوات المساواة في ما يصلح رأس المال. (عيني) لا العرض: أي لو وهب لأحدهما عرض أو ورثه فهو له، ولا تبطل به المفاوضة وكذا العقار؛ لأنه لا تصح الشركة في هذه الأشياء، فالتفاوت فيه لا يمنع ابتداء، فكذا بقاء، ولا يشترط المساواة فيه، ولو ورث أحدهما ديناً وهو دراهم أو دنانير لا تبطل المفاوضة حتى يقبض. (مستخلص عيني)
And it becomes invalid if one of them is given a gift, or inherits something, or borrows (?) the mufawada partnership, and it becomes 'inan. That is, if one of the two partners is given a gift, or inherits, or something reaches his hand in which partnership is valid, like gold, silver, bullion, and current flus, the mufawada becomes invalid and becomes an 'inan partnership, because the objective—equality in wealth—is lost. However, it turns into 'inan because that is possible, for in 'inan equality is not a condition. (Mukhtalish, Fath) 'if one of them is given a gift': meaning one of the mufawadites, and he accepts and takes possession. (Tahaawi) 'in which partnership is valid': that is, gold and silver, because equality is lost in that which is suitable as capital. (Ayni) 'not for goods': meaning if one is given goods as a gift or inherits them, it is his, and the mufawada does not become invalid by that, and likewise real estate, because partnership is not valid in these things, so disparity in them does not prevent initially, and thus also in continuance, and equality is not conditioned in them. And if one inherits a debt which is dirhams or dinars, the mufawada does not become invalid until he collects it. (Mukhtalish, Ayni)
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage discusses when mufawada partnership becomes invalid due to unequal property acquisition. The ruling states that if one partner receives a gift or inheritance of property that is suitable as capital for a partnership (i.e., gold, silver, bullion, or current flus), the mufawada invalidates and automatically becomes an 'inan partnership because the condition of equality is lost. However, if the property received is in kind (goods) or real estate, mufawada remains valid because partnership cannot be based on such items, so inequality does not affect it. Similarly, if a debt is inherited, invalidity occurs only upon actual collection. The conversion to 'inan is possible because 'inan does not require equality.

Key Terms
عنان (ʿinān): A limited partnership where partners contribute capital but are not guarantors for each other; equality is not required.
نقدان (naqdān): Gold and silver coinage (dinars and dirhams) that serve as capital for partnership.
عرض (ʿarḍ): Goods, commodities, or merchandise; not suitable as partnership capital in mufawada.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A herd of sheep
B) A house
C) A debt of 1000 dirhams not yet collected
D) A chest of silver coins

Key Takeaway
Mufawada invalidates only when a partner receives capital-suitable property (gold/silver/bullion/current flus); goods, real estate, and uncollected debts do not affect it, and the partnership automatically converts to 'inan.

Permissible Capital Forms for Mufawada and 'Inan
Ruling on the required form of capital for valid partnership contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on permissible capital forms for mufawada and 'inan partnerships, identify the evidence from the hadith against using goods, and distinguish the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on flus.

Core Idea
Partnership capital must be monetary — goods are invalid because they lead to profit on unguaranteed wealth.

This lesson examines the essential condition of capital type in mufawada and 'inan partnerships. The Hanafi school strictly requires capital to be in the form of gold, silver, bullion, or current copper coins. Why are goods like wheat or livestock not acceptable? The answer lies in a key hadith forbidding profit on what is not guaranteed. We will also explore the internal Hanafi disagreement on copper coins and how custom influenced the final relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the exact ruling: mufawada and 'inan partnerships are not valid except with dirhams, dinars, bullion, and current flus. Note that goods ('urud) are excluded.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognise that Imam Malik permits partnership with goods of the same type, as well as with measured and weighed items, because the capital is known.
Malik's 'urud includes commodities like cloth, grains, etc.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand Malik's qiyas: a contract on known capital resembles currency contracts; goods are known capital, so the contract is valid.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Study the Hanafi rebuttal using the hadith: if partners contribute goods and sell them, the profit from the price difference involves earning from what is not guaranteed (the partner's goods before purchase). This is forbidden.
The hadith 'profit on what is not guaranteed' is the core proof.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and intra-Hanafi nuance
The practical difference: Malik allows goods; Hanafis restrict to monetary items. Also note the internal Hanafi split on flus: Muhammad permits them (relied upon), while Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf do not due to their temporary conventional status.
The chosen view for flus is Muhammad's due to widespread custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تصح أي لا تنعقد هاتان الشركتان إلا بالدراهم والدنانير والتبر والفلوس النافقة، وقال مالك: يجوز الشركة بالعروض إذا اتحد الجنس، وكذا بالمكيل والموزون؛ لأنها عقدت على رأس معلوم، فأشبه النقود ، بخلاف المضاربة؛ لأن القياس يأباها ؛ لما فيه من ربح ما لم يضمن، ومال المضاربة غير مضمون على المضارب، وقد نهى النبي عليه عن ربح ما لم يضمن، فاقتصر على مورد الشرع أي عقد المضاربة. قلنا: هذا يؤدي إلى ربح ما لم يضمن ؛ لأنه إذا باع كل واحد منهما رأس ماله وتفاضل الثمنان، فما يستحقه الزيادة في مال صاحبه ما لم يضمن وما لم يملك بخلاف النقدين؛ لأنه ما يشتريه أحدهما يدخل في ملكهما، وثمنه في ذمته، يرجع به على صاحبه بحسابه؛ إذ النقدين لا يتعينان، فكان ما يضمن، وأما التبر، فقيل: لأن الذهب والفضة خلقا ثمنين فيجوز العقد به، وقيل: لا يجوز إلا بالتعامل؛ لأنها بالتعامل تلحق بالأثمان وهو الأصح، وأما الفلوس النافقة فلأنها ألمان فأخذت حكم النقدين، وقيل: هذا عند محمد؛ لأنها ملحقة عنده بالنقود حتى لا يجوز بيع الاثنين لواحد بأعيانها، فأختير قوله لمكان العرف وعند الشيخين: لا تصح الشركة فيها ولا المضاربة؛ لأن رواجها عارض باصطلاح الناس. (مستخلص، عيني) والتبر: وهو القطعة المأخوذة من المعدن، أي الذي يضرب من ذهب وفضة. (ط، عيني)
And it is not valid—i.e., these two partnerships are not contracted except with dirhams, dinars, bullion, and current flus. And Malik said: partnership with goods is permissible if the type is uniform, and similarly with measured and weighed items, because it is contracted on a known capital, so it resembles currency, unlike mudarabah, because analogy opposes it due to the element of profit on what is not guaranteed, and the capital in mudarabah is not guaranteed by the mudarib, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade profit on what is not guaranteed, so it was confined to the occasion of the law, i.e., the mudarabah contract. We say: this leads to profit on what is not guaranteed, because when each sells his capital and the two prices differ, what one gets as excess in his partner's wealth is not guaranteed and not owned; unlike with currencies, because what one purchases enters their joint ownership, and its price is in his liability, he recovers from his partner according to his share, since currencies are not specific, so they are guaranteed. As for bullion, it is said that gold and silver were created as prices, so the contract with them is permissible; and it is said it is not permissible except by custom, because by custom they are considered prices, and this is the more correct view. As for current flus, because they are money-like, they take the ruling of currencies. It is said this is the view of Muhammad, because he attaches them to currencies such that selling two for one is not permitted in their specific form; thus his view is chosen due to custom. According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the two masters), partnership and mudarabah are not valid with them, because their currency status is temporary based on people's convention. (Mukhtalish, Ayni) 'al-tibr' (bullion): it is the piece taken from the mine, i.e., that is struck from gold and silver. (Tahaawi, Ayni)
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage explains the required form of capital for mufawada and 'inan partnerships. The Hanafi position is that only dirhams, dinars, bullion (tibr), and current flus (fulus) are valid as capital. Goods ('urud) are not permitted as joint capital. The passage includes an inter-madhhab dispute with Malik, who allows partnership with goods of the same type. The Hanafi response uses the hadith forbidding profit on what is not guaranteed, arguing that if partners contribute goods, selling them leads to profiting from each other's property without guarantee. For bullion, the stronger view is its permissibility by custom. For flus, there is an intra-Hanafi disagreement: Muhammad permits them as effective currency, while Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf forbid them due to their conventional nature. The chosen position is Muhammad's view due to widespread custom.

Key Terms
تبر (tibr): Bullion, i.e., raw gold or silver from the mine, not yet coined but treated as a price commodity by custom.
فلوس نافقة (fulūs nāfiqah): Current copper coins that are circulated as money by people. They take the ruling of currencies in Muhammad's view.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Gold dinars
B) Silver dirhams
C) A hundred kilograms of wheat
D) Current copper coins (flus)

Key Takeaway
For valid mufawada and 'inan partnerships, Hanafis require capital exclusively in the form of monetary items (gold, silver, bullion, or current flus); goods are invalid due to the hadith forbidding profit on unguaranteed wealth.

Capital Requirements for Mufawadah and 'Inan Partnerships
Explaining that partnership requires specific currencies and rebutting Malik's view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the valid capital types for Hanafi partnerships, explain the proof from the hadith on profit from unguaranteed property, and distinguish the Hanafi view from Malik's position.

Core Idea
Partnership is valid only with gold, silver, uncoined bullion, or current copper coins — commodities are excluded to avoid ribh ma lam yudman.

In this passage, the author lays down the foundational condition for two key partnership types: mufawadah and 'inan. The capital must be of a specific nature — currency or its equivalent — to ensure that any profit derived is from guaranteed property. The ruling is defended by the Prophetic prohibition of earning profit from what is not guaranteed, and the passage also addresses an objection from Imam Malik, who permits partnerships with commodities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what is being ruled upon (mufawadah and 'inan) and the hukm level (valid only with specific capital).
The text states 'lā taṣiḥḥ' meaning the contracts are not valid unless conditions are met.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proof cited: the hadith 'forbidding profit from what is not guaranteed'.
The hadith is not given with chain; it is a known principle.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Understand how the hadith proves the ruling: profit must come from property for which one bears liability.
Money is non-specific and thus becomes guaranteed when used in partnership; commodities remain specific and unguaranteed.
4
Note the ikhtilaf with Malik
Malik allows partnership with commodities if the type is same. The Hanafi response shows that this would violate the hadith.
The response uses the same principle to reject Malik's analogy.
5
Evaluate the internal Hanafi ikhtilaf on fulus
Imam Muhammad permits current fulus due to custom; Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf restrict to gold and silver. The author states that Muhammad's view is chosen for fatwa because of custom.
This shows the role of custom ('urf) in tarjih.

It is not valid, meaning these two partnerships (mufawadah and 'inan) are only contracted with dirhams, dinars, tibr (uncoined gold/silver), and current fulus. Malik said: Partnership with commodities (al-‘urud) is valid if the type is the same, and likewise with measured and weighed items, because it is contracted on known capital, so it resembles money. This is contrary to mudarabah because analogy rejects it due to the issue of profit from what is not guaranteed, and the property of mudarabah is not guaranteed by the mudarib, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade profit from what is not guaranteed, so they limited it to what is legislated. We (Hanafis) say: This leads to profit from what is not guaranteed because when each sells his capital and the prices differ, the excess becomes profit from the property of his partner without guarantee and without ownership, unlike with money because what one buys enters into the joint ownership and its price is in his liability, from which he can recover from his partner; since money is non-specific, it becomes guaranteed. As for tibr, its profit: some say because gold and silver are inherently prices, so contracting with them is valid; some say it is not valid except by custom because custom attaches to them as prices, and this is the correct view. Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage discusses the capital requirement for mufawadah and 'inan partnerships in the Hanafi school. The text explicitly states that both types of partnership are only valid when formed with dirhams (silver coins), dinars (gold coins), tibr (uncoined gold or silver), and current fulus (copper coins in circulation). This is a ruling based on the prohibition of earning profit from unguaranteed property (ribh ma lam yudman). The Hanafis respond to Imam Malik's view allowing partnership with commodities by arguing that such an arrangement would lead to earning profit from property that is not guaranteed by the other partner. When the partnership capital consists of money, property purchased becomes joint and the price is a debt, so profit arises from guaranteed property. Tibr is included because gold and silver are inherently established as prices. For fulus, there is internal disagreement: Imam Muhammad allows partnership with current fulus because they are treated like money due to custom, and this view is preferred for fatwa, while Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf disallow it because fulus are not real currency but only a conventional medium. The passage concludes with a definition of tibr.

Key Terms
المفاوضة (al-mufāwaḍah): A partnership where each partner contributes equal capital, has equal right to act in trade, and is responsible for the other's debts (mutual guarantee).
العنان (al-‘inān): A partnership where partners may have unequal capital or profit shares, and only agency (wakāla) is included, not mutual guarantee.
التبر (al-tibr): Uncoined gold or silver, i.e., raw metal extracted from the mine, not yet minted into coins.
الفلوس النافقة (al-fulūs al-nāfiqah): Copper coins that are currently accepted as legal tender and in circulation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Any type of property as long as it is evaluated.

Key Takeaway
Partnership capital in the Hanafi school must consist of gold, silver, uncoined bullion, or current copper coins; other property invalidates the contract.

Effect of Gift and Inheritance on Mufawadah Partnership
Ruling that mufawadah becomes 'inan upon receipt of capital-eligible property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that receipt of capital-eligible property by one partner in a mufawadah transforms it into an 'inan partnership, and distinguish which types of property trigger this transformation.

Core Idea
A mufawadah partnership becomes 'inan when one partner receives exclusive ownership of capital-eligible property.

Mufawadah requires absolute equality in capital. If one partner receives property through gift, inheritance, charity, or bequest that can serve as partnership capital, that equality is lost. However, the partnership is not voided; it automatically converts into an 'inan partnership where inequality is permitted. This lesson unpacks the conditions and exceptions to this rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the property received
Determine whether the property falls into the category of 'capital-eligible' (naqdayn, tibr, current fulus) or other (commodities, real estate, debt).
Capital-eligible means it can be used as partnership capital in a valid shirkah.
2
Apply the ruling for capital-eligible property
If the received property is capital-eligible, the mufawadah is invalidated (as mufawadah) and becomes an 'inan partnership automatically.
The partnership is not dissolved; it continues under a different form.
3
Apply the ruling for non-capital-eligible property
If the received property is a commodity, real estate, or debt (until collected), the mufawadah remains valid. The inequality does not affect it because such property cannot be part of the partnership capital.
Debt is treated as non-capital until actually collected.
4
Understand the rationale
The ruling rests on the fact that mufawadah requires complete equality in capital. Any exclusive addition breaks that equality, but the law allows transformation into 'inan where equality is not required.
This is a manifestation of the Hanafi principle of facility (taysir) in partnerships.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتبطل إلخ: لو قال المصنف بدل قوله: "إن وهب لأحدهما أو ورث" "إن ملك أحدهما" لكان أخصر وأشمل ما لو وصل إلى يده بصدقة أو وصية أي إذا وهب أحد الشريكين أو ورث أو وصل إلى يده ما تصح فيه الشركة كالنقدين والتبر والفلس النافقين بطلت المفاوضة، وصارت شركة عنان؛ لفوات المقصود، وهو المساواة في المال إلا أنه ينقلب عناناً للإمكان، فإن فيه المساواة ليس بشرط. إن وهب لأحدهما: أي أحد المتفاوضين وقبل وقبض. تصح فيه الشركة: وهو النقدان لفوات المساواة في ما يصلح رأس المال. لا العرض: أي لو وهب لأحدهما عرض أو ورثه فهو له، ولا تبطل به المفاوضة وكذا العقار؛ لأنه لا تصح الشركة في هذه الأشياء، فالتفاوت فيه لا يمنع ابتداء، فكذا بقاء، ولا يشترط المساواة فيه، ولو ورث أحدهما ديناً وهو دراهم أو دنانير لا تبطل المفاوضة حتى يقبض.
And it is invalidated, etc.: If the author had said instead of his statement 'if one of them is given or inherits' 'if one of them owns', it would be more concise and comprehensive, including if something reaches his hand by charity or bequest. That is: if one of the two partners is given as a gift or inherits or something that qualifies for partnership as capital (like cash, tibr, and current fulus) reaches his hand, the mufawadah is invalidated and becomes an 'inan partnership due to the loss of the intended equality in capital, but it turns into 'inan because that is possible, since in 'inan equality is not a condition. 'If one of them is given': meaning one of the two partners in mufawadah, and he accepted and took possession. 'Something valid for partnership in it': which is the two currencies (naqdayn), because equality is lost in what is suitable as capital. 'Not a commodity': meaning if one of them is given a commodity or inherits it, it is his and does not invalidate the mufawadah; similarly real estate, because partnership in these things is not valid, so disparity in them does not prevent initially, so likewise in continuation, and equality in them is not a condition. If one of them inherits a debt that is dirhams or dinars, the mufawadah is not invalidated until he collects it.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling when a partner in a mufawadah partnership receives ownership of new property through gift, inheritance, charity, or bequest. If that property is of a type that is eligible to be partnership capital (i.e., naqdayn, tibr, or current fulus), then the mufawadah becomes invalid and automatically transforms into an 'inan partnership. This is because mufawadah requires absolute equality in capital, so any new addition that goes to only one partner breaks that equality. However, the partnership is not dissolved but becomes an 'inan, because equality is not required in 'inan. If the received property is a commodity (like cloth) or real estate, it does not affect the mufawadah at all, because such items are not eligible for partnership capital anyway, so the inequality has no effect. Similarly, if a partner inherits a debt of money, the mufawadah remains valid until the debt is actually collected, because until then it is not actual wealth.

Key Terms
المفاوضة (al-mufāwaḍah): A partnership with full equality in capital, action, and guarantee; receipt of exclusive property invalidates it.
العنان (al-‘inān): A partnership that allows inequality; it is the default transformation from invalidated mufawadah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The mufawadah becomes 'inan immediately because the inherited money is capital-eligible.
B) The mufawadah remains valid until the debt is actually collected, then becomes 'inan.
C) The mufawadah is completely dissolved.
D) The inherited debt is shared with the other partner equally.

Key Takeaway
Receipt of capital-eligible property by one mufawadah partner transforms the partnership into 'inan, while commodities, real estate, and uncollected debts do not affect it.

Shirkah — Partnership

Legal Stratagem to Form a Partnership in Commodities
Explaining a permissible stratagem to form partnership in commodities via sale and contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi stratagem for forming a partnership in commodities and explain why it makes the profit permissible.

Core Idea
Profit must arise from guaranteed property; a preliminary sale ensures this.

In Hanafi fiqh, direct contribution of commodities into a partnership without prior sale renders the partnership invalid because the profit would arise from unguaranteed property. This lesson explains a lawful stratagem (hila) that overcomes this obstacle: each partner sells half of his commodity to the other, creating a guaranteed debt, then they form the partnership. By the end, you will understand the reasoning and the conditions for this stratagem.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Problem
Understand why forming a partnership directly with commodities (‘urūḍ) is problematic: profit must come from property that is guaranteed (mudman), and without prior sale, the contributed commodities remain unguaranteed in the hands of the other partner.
The underlying principle is that profit follows liability (al-kharāj bi al-ḍamān).
2
Examine the Stratagem
Each partner sells half of his commodity to the other at a price (e.g., half of his goods for a sum that becomes a debt). This sale creates joint ownership (shirkat al-milk) and makes each partner liable for the price of the other’s half, thus the property becomes guaranteed.
The price can be in dirhams or a commodity; it becomes a debt on the buyer.
3
Form the Partnership Contract
After the sale, the partners contract an ‘inān partnership (shirkat al-‘aqd) using the now jointly-owned and guaranteed assets. The contract grants agency to each other, allowing them to trade with the combined capital.
The partnership must meet the usual conditions of ‘inān: it can include agency only, not mutual guarantee.
4
Analyze the Legal Effect
The profit generated from trading with the combined assets is profit from property that each partner is liable for, thus satisfying the requirement. The stratagem is valid (ṣaḥīḥ) and agreed upon within the Hanafi school, though other schools may not require it.
5
Recognize the Scope and Limitations
The stratagem works for any commodities, not just those of the same type. It is not an evasion but a careful application of the principles. The partnership must be properly formed after the sale.
The text mentions that even if the sale is for dirhams and then partnership in the same commodities, it is also permissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بنصف عرض الآخر، وعقد الشركة صح، وعنان إن تضمنت وكالة فقط، أي بعد البيع أي والثانية شركة عنان أي الشركة وتصح مع التساوي في المال دون الربح، وعكسه، وببعض المال، وخلاف أي شركة العنان الجنس، دون التساوي في الربح عرض الآخر حتى صار مال كل واحد منهما مشتركا بينهما شركة ملك. With half of the other's commodity, and the partnership contract is valid, and it is 'inan if it includes only agency. (Meaning: after the sale, and the second type is 'inan partnership.) And it is valid with equality in capital without equality in profit, and the opposite, and with part of wealth, and with different types, i.e., 'inan partnership. The other's commodity until each partner's property becomes jointly owned as a partnership of ownership. (Commentary) It is valid because by the sale he became his partner in ownership, so that neither can dispose of the other's share, then by the subsequent contract it becomes a partnership of contract, so each can dispose of the other's share. This is a stratagem for one who wants partnership in commodities; because by that half of each partner's commodity becomes guaranteed on the other partner by the price, so the profit resulting from the two capitals is profit from what is guaranteed, and it is permissible. And his statement: 'with half of the other's commodity' is agreed upon; because if he sold it to him for dirhams and then formed the partnership in the commodities he sold, it would also be permissible.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage presents a legal stratagem (hila) to validly form a partnership in commodities (al-‘urūḍ), which would otherwise be invalid if attempted directly. The method: Each partner sells half of his commodity to the other partner in exchange for a price (which becomes a debt). Then, after this sale, the two partners form a partnership contract (shirkat al-‘aqd) using the commodities in their possession. At this point, each owns half of the other's original commodity through the sale, and this mutual ownership is guaranteed because of the price debt. Thus, when they subsequently trade with the combined assets, the profit arises from property that is guaranteed (mudman), satisfying the condition that profit should only be from guaranteed property. The resulting partnership is an 'inan partnership if it contains only agency (wakāla) without mutual guarantee (kafāla). This stratagem is endorsed by the Hanafi school and effectively allows partnership in physical goods.

Key Terms
الحيلة (al-ḥīlah): A lawful stratagem to achieve a permissible result that would otherwise be blocked by a legal obstacle.
شركة ملك (shirkat al-milk): Partnership in ownership (joint ownership) that arises from sale, inheritance, etc., where each co-owner can only dispose of his own share.
الربح من المضمون (al-ribḥ min al-maḍmūn): Profit arising from guaranteed property; a condition for the validity of partnerships in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) To avoid paying zakat on the capital.
C) To bypass the condition of equal capital in 'inan partnership.
D) To make the partnership eligible for mudarabah rules.

Key Takeaway
A lawful sale before the partnership contract transforms commodities into guaranteed property, making the partnership valid according to the Hanafi school.

Shirkah — Partnership

Flexibility of Profit Distribution in 'Inan Partnership and the Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute
Stating the permissibility of diverse profit-sharing arrangements in 'inan partnership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on profit-sharing in 'inan partnership and evaluate the opposing arguments of Zufar, al-Shafi'i, and Malik.

Core Idea
Profit in 'inan partnership depends on agreement; loss follows capital proportion. Work justifies unequal profit.

This passage delves into the 'inan partnership, a flexible arrangement where partners may contribute different amounts of capital and still share profits unequally. The Hanafi school permits this based on mutual agreement, as long as both partners are actively involved. However, Zufar, al-Shafi'i, and Malik insist that profit must be strictly proportional to capital. We will explore the Hanafi proof and the practical consequence of this difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key features of 'inan partnership
Note that 'inan includes only agency (wakāla), not guarantee (kafāla). Partners act as agents for each other but are not liable for each other's debts.
Compare with mufawadah which includes both agency and guarantee.
2
Understand the Hanafi ruling on profit distribution
The Hanafi school allows partners to agree on any profit share, even if it does not match capital ratios, as long as both partners work. It is invalid to give excess profit to a non-working partner.
This flexibility is a key feature distinguishing 'inan from other partnerships.
3
Examine the opposing view
Zufar (a Hanafi jurist), al-Shafi'i, and Malik argue that profit must be proportional to capital because any excess is profit from unguaranteed property.
Their argument hinges on the principle that profit follows liability (al-kharaj bi al-daman).
4
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi rely on the statement of 'Ali (ra): 'Profit is according to what they condition; loss is according to capital.' They also analogize to mudarabah, where the manager earns profit through work without capital.
The proof is a combination of a prophetic saying (albeit mawquf) and qiyas.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: Under Hanafi ruling, partners with unequal capital can agree on equal profit share. Under the opposing view, profit must be divided in exact capital proportion.
The Hanafi position is the mufta bihi in the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعنان إن تضمنت وكالة فقط، وتصح إلخ : أي يجوز أن يتساويا في المال ويتفاضلا في الربح أو تفاضلا في المال وتساويا في الربح، ومعناه: أن يشترط الأكثر للعامل منهما أو لأكثرهما عملاً، وإن شرطاه للقاعد أو لأقلهما عملاً، فلا يجوز، وقال زفر: يستحقان الربح على قدر مالهما ولا يجوز أن يشترطا خلاف ذلك؛ لأنه يؤدي إلى ربح ما لم يضمن، وبه قال الشافعي ومالك، ولنا : قوله علي : الربح على ما شرطا والوضيعة على قدر المالين من غير فصل بين التساوي والتفاضل. [ رمز الحقائق: ٤٦٦/١] ، ولأن الربح كما يستحق بالمال يستحق بالعمل أيضاً كما في المضاربة، وقد يكون أحدهما أحذق من الآخر وأقدر على العمل فلا يرضى بالمساواة فمست الحاجة إلى التفاضل. (مستخلص) وعكسه: أي وتصح هذه الشركة أيضاً مع عکسه وهو أن يتساويا في الربح دون المال. (عيني) وببعض المال: أي أن يعقد كل واحد منهما ببعض ماله دون البعض، بخلاف المفاوضة؛ لأن التساوي ليس بشرط في العنان، وقوله: "وخلاف الجنس" أي يصح هذا العقد باختلاف جنسي الشريكين بأن كان أحدهما جنس دراهم وجنس الآخر دنانير، وقوله: "وعدم الخلط" أي يصح العقد أيضاً مع عدم الخلط، وقال زفر والشافعي: -
And 'inan: if it includes only agency, and it is valid... i.e., it is permissible for them (the partners) to be equal in capital and differ in profit, or differ in capital and be equal in profit. Its meaning: that they stipulate more for the one who works from them or for the one who does more work, but if they stipulate it for the inactive one or for the one with less work, it is not permissible. Zufar said: They deserve profit according to their capital, and it is not permissible for them to stipulate otherwise, because it leads to profit from what is not guaranteed. This is also the view of Shafi'i and Malik. Our (Hanafi) proof: the statement of 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him): 'Profit is according to what they condition, and loss is according to the proportion of capital,' without distinction between equality and difference. And also because profit is deserved by capital as well as by work, as in mudarabah. One partner may be more skillful and capable of work, so he would not be satisfied with equality; thus, there is need for difference. And its opposite: i.e., this partnership is also valid with its opposite, which is equality in profit without equality in capital. And with part of wealth: i.e., each contracts with some of his property, not all, unlike mufawadah because equality is not a condition in 'inan. And his statement: 'and difference of type' i.e., this contract is valid with different types of the two partners, such as one having dirhams and the other dinars. And his statement: 'and lack of mixing' i.e., the contract is valid even without mixing (of capital). And Zufar and Shafi'i said: -
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage details the rules of the 'inan partnership. It begins by noting that 'inan includes only agency (wakāla) and not mutual guarantee (kafāla). The key feature is its flexibility: partners may contribute equal or unequal capital, and they may agree to share profits in any proportion, even if unequal to capital contribution. However, they cannot stipulate profit for a partner who does not work at all or does less work if the agreement gives him more profit than his work warrants. The Hanafi school relies on the statement attributed to 'Ali that profit is based on agreement, while loss is proportional to capital. This is contrasted with the view of Zufar, Shafi'i, and Malik, who insist that profit must be in proportion to capital, just like loss. The Hanafis argue that profit can also be earned by work, as in mudarabah, and that practical needs require flexibility when partners have differing skills. Additionally, the 'inan partnership is valid even when partners invest different currencies (e.g., one dirhams and the other dinars) and even when their capitals are not mixed together physically. The text breaks off while discussing the disagreement with Zufar and Shafi'i on this point.

Key Terms
شركة العنان (shirkat al-‘inān): A partnership that includes only agency (wakāla) without mutual guarantee; capital and profit may be unequal.
الوكالة (al-wakālah): Agency: each partner acts as an agent for the other in trade, but is not a guarantor for his debts.
الوضيعة (al-waḍī‘ah): Loss incurred in the partnership; it is always distributed according to capital proportion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, in an 'inan partnership, what is the condition for allowing profit distribution that is not proportional to capital?
A) Both partners must contribute equal capital.
B) Both partners must be actively working in the business.
C) The profit share must be equal to the capital ratio.
D) The partners must have different currencies.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, profit in an 'inan partnership is based on mutual agreement, not capital proportion, as long as both partners contribute work.

Shirkah — Partnership
intermediate
12 min
Rulings on Partnership Without Mixing Capital and Effect of Destruction
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mixing and destruction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the requirement of mixing capitals in partnership and apply the ruling on the effect of capital destruction on the partnership contract, including the role of explicit agency.

Core Idea
Partnership in Hanafi law is a contract of agency; mixing capitals is not required, and capital destruction voids the contract unless agency is explicit.

This lesson explores two interlinked issues central to Hanafi partnership law: whether physical mixing of capitals is a condition for a valid partnership, and what happens when a partner's capital is destroyed before any purchase. You will examine the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and his two main disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, and understand the practical consequences of their disagreement.

Guided Path
1
State Abu Hanifa's position on mixing
Identify the core claim: partnership is a contract of agency (ʻaqd tawkīl) and does not require physical mixing of capitals. Profit is earned through effort and contract, not solely from capital.
This is the mufta bihi view within the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the Sahibayn's opposing view
State that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani require mixing because partnership is defined by mixing of capitals, and profit follows capital.
They base their view on the definition of shirkah as mixing and the principle that profit is a branch of capital.
3
Analyze the proofs and responses
Examine the dalil for each side. Abu Hanifa uses the analogy to mudaraba and the fungibility of dirhams/dinars. The Sahibayn rely on the necessary link between capital and profit.
The dispute hinges on the nature of partnership: contract vs. property mixing.
4
Apply the ruling to capital destruction
Determine the effect of destruction of one or both capitals before purchase. The partnership becomes void unless explicit agency was stated in the contract, in which case the agency survives and subsequent purchases are shared.
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between al-Hasan ibn Ziyad (ownership partnership) and Muhammad (contract partnership) after destruction.
5
Evaluate the condition of fixed profit (musammah)
Understand that stipulating a fixed profit amount corrupts the condition but not the entire partnership. The condition is void, and profit is redistributed according to capital shares.
This illustrates the principle that corrupt conditions (shurūṭ fāsida) do not invalidate the contract in Hanafi law.

(مستخلص، عيني) وعدم الخلط : أي وتصح أيضاً بعدم الخلط بين المالين (عيني) فقط أي لا يطالب الشريك الآخر؛ لأنها (عيني) ورجع على شريكه أي إن كان قد أدى الثمن من مال نفسه، وهذا القيد أهمله وكذا القدوري للعلم به من قوله: "يرجع لظهور أنه لا يكون إلا إذا أداه من مال نفسه؛ لأنه وكيل من جهته، فإذا نقده من مال نفسه يرجع عليه، فإن كان لا يعرف ذلك إلا بقوله فعليه الحجة؛ لأنه يدعي وجوب المال في ذمة الآخر، والآخر ينكر، والقول قوله، وإن كان نقده من مال مشترك لا يرجع عليه، ولو ادعى بعد الهلاك أنه اشتراه للشركة كان عليه البيان [ رمز الحقائق: ٤٦٧/١] (فتح) وتبطل بهلاك المالين: أي مال الشريكين؛ لأنه هو المعقود عليه في الشركة، فإذا هلك بطل العقد كما في الهبة والوصية والبيع، يبطل العقد بهلاك المعقود عليه بخلاف الوكالة المفردة والمضاربة؛ لأنه لا يتعين الثمنان فيهما بالتعيين، وإنما يتعينان بالقبض. وقوله: "أو أحدهما" أي تبطل أيضاً بهلاك أحد المالين؛ لأن الشريك لم يرض بالآخر إلا ليشركه هو في ماله فإذا هلك أحدهما فات ذلك، ففات رضاه بشركته فيبطل العقد، وأي المالين هلك، هلك من مال مالكه، فإن هلك في يده فظاهر، وإن هلك في يد الشريك فكذلك؛ لأنه أمانة، وإنما قال: "قبل الشراء"؛ لأنه إذا هلك أحد المالين بعد الشراء بالمال الآخر كان المشترى مشتركاً بينهما كما في المسألة
لا تتضمن كفالة.

الآخر: أي الشريك الآخر بعد الاشتراك. (ط) ورجع بحصته إلخ وهذه الشركة شركة ملك عند الحسن بن زياد، فلا يجوز لكل واحد منهما أن يتصرف إلا في نصيبه؛ لأن شركة العقد بطلت بهلاك أحد المالين، وعندمحمد : شركة عقد حتى يجوز لكل واحد منهما التصرف فيه؛ لأنه حين وقع وقع مشتركا بينهما بشركة عقد، فلا تبطل بالهلاك بعد تقرره ولو قال المصنف : "فهلك" بالفاء ليدل على التعقيب لكان أولى؛ لأنه إذا هلك مال أحدهما ثم اشترى الآخر بماله إن صرحا بالوكالة في عقد الشركة فالمشترى مشترك بينهما على ما شرطا؛ لأن عقد الشركة إن بطل بالهلاك، فالوكالة المصرح بها باقية، فكان المشترى مشتركاً بينهما بحكم الوكالة المفردة، ويرجع عليه بحصته من الثمن، وإن ذكرا مجرد الشركة ولم يصرحا بالوكالة، فهو للمشتري خاصة؛ لأن دخوله في ملكه بحكم الوكالة التي في ضمن الشركة، وقد بطلت الشركة بهلاك المال، فيبطل ما في ضمنها، بخلاف ما إذا صر حا بها؛ لأنها صارت مقصودة. Book of Partnership. Rulings of Partnership. And without mixing. And the buyer is demanded only for the price, and he recovers from his partner his share of it, and his limit is from the partner of 'inan who bought it because he is the contracting party. And it becomes void by the destruction of both capitals or one of them before purchase, and if one of them bought something with his money, and it perished... The partnership absolutely, i.e., or the destruction of the last of the two capitals, i.e., one of the two partners, i.e., with his own money, the price. It is not valid in both; because profit is a branch of capital and partnership in it is not conceivable except after the occurrence of partnership in the principal, and that is not conceivable without mixing of the two capitals, and the two species do not mix, and the basis in that is that partnership is an expression of mixing according to them, and upon this they base the condition of unity of species, the impermissibility of disparity in profit with equality in capital, and the impermissibility of partnership of acceptance and faces. And for us: partnership is a contract of agency from both parties so that each buys with a price on his liability on the condition that the purchased is between them, and this does not require mixing, and profit is deserved by the contract as it is deserved by capital. And it is said: this issue is based on the fact that dirhams and dinars become specific according to them like commodities, and according to us they do not become specific; because profit is not obtained by capital but rather by action; because he is principal in half and agent in half, so if partnership in action is realized without mixing, it is realized in what is obtained by action, which is profit, without mixing as well, and it becomes like mudaraba, so unity of species is not required nor equality in profit, and the partnership of acceptance and faces is valid. (Mukhtasar, Ayni) And without mixing: i.e., it is also valid without mixing between the two capitals (Ayni). Only, i.e., the other partner is not demanded; because it (Ayni) and he recovers from his partner, i.e., if he had paid the price from his own money, and this restriction is neglected by him and likewise al-Quduri due to being known from his statement: 'He recovers because it is apparent that it only occurs if he paid it from his own money; because he is an agent on his behalf, so if he paid from his own money he recovers from him, and if it is not known except by his statement, then upon him is the proof; because he claims the obligation of money in the liability of the other, and the other denies, and the statement is his, and if he paid from joint money, he does not recover, and if after destruction he claimed that he bought it for the partnership, he must provide evidence. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/467] (Fath) And it becomes void by the destruction of both capitals: i.e., the capital of the two partners; because it is the object of the contract in partnership, so if it perishes the contract becomes void as in gift, bequest, and sale, the contract becomes void by destruction of the object of contract contrary to isolated agency and mudaraba; because the two prices do not become specific by specification in them, but rather become specific by possession. (Ayni) Of the profit, such that one of them says: I have from the profit one hundred dirhams for example, then the remainder is divided. (Ayni) This means: if one of them stipulates specific dirhams from the profit, the partnership becomes corrupt; because it is a condition that necessitates termination of the partnership; since perhaps the amount they named for one of them may not be obtained, and by this condition the partnership is nullified, so it is apparent that what is intended is the corruption of the condition; because the partnership is not invalidated by corrupt conditions, so if the condition becomes void, the profit is according to the capital. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses two interconnected issues: (1) whether mixing of capitals is a condition for the validity of a partnership, and (2) the effect of destruction of capital before purchase. The author first states the position of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), who require mixing because partnership is defined by mixed ownership. They argue that profit follows capital, so partnership in profit cannot exist without partnership in the capital, which requires mixing. They also require unity of type and equal profit for equal capital. The Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa) is presented as a counter-argument: partnership is a contract of agency (عقد توكيل) where each partner buys on credit for the partnership, and profit is earned through the contract and effort, not solely through capital. This is analogous to mudaraba, where mixing is not required. The text then discusses the scenario where one partner buys something with his own money after the destruction of the other's capital. If the capital perishes before the purchase, the partnership contract becomes void because the object of the contract is gone. However, if the purchase occurs after destruction, the ruling depends on whether agency was explicitly stated. If so, the purchase is shared; if not, it belongs solely to the buyer. The text also notes an intra-Hanafi disagreement: al-Hasan ibn Ziyad considers it a partnership of ownership (ملكية) after destruction, while Imam Muhammad considers it still a contract partnership. The preferred view (mufta bihi) is that the partnership continues if agency was explicit. Finally, the passage addresses a condition stipulating a fixed amount of profit (مسماة). Such a condition corrupts the partnership because it implies the partner is guaranteed profit regardless of actual outcome, which undermines the partnership's nature. However, the condition itself is void, not the entire partnership; the profit is then redistributed according to capital shares.

Key Terms
شركة (shirkah): A contract of partnership where two or more persons agree to share profits from a joint business.
خلط (khalṭ): Physical mixing of capitals, e.g., pooling money in a common fund.
عقد توكيل (ʻaqd tawkīl): A contract of agency, where each partner authorizes the other to act on his behalf.
مسماة (musammāh): A specified fixed amount of profit stipulated for one partner, e.g., 100 dirhams from the profit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a partnership contract is formed without explicit mention of agency, and one partner's capital is destroyed before any purchase, the surviving partner buys goods with his own money. According to the Hanafi school (mufta bihi), the purchased goods are:
A) Shared because the partnership was valid.
B) Solely for the buyer because the partnership is void and no agency remains.
C) Shared because agency is always implied in partnership.
D) Void because the contract is void.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits partnership without mixing capitals, as it is fundamentally an agency contract; however, destruction of capital before purchase voids the partnership unless agency is explicitly stated.

Permissible Transactions for a Partner: Bida'a, Hiring, Agency, Mudaraba
Lists permissible transactions for a partner beyond core business.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which transactions a partner may undertake with partnership capital beyond the core business, and identify the exception regarding lending.

Core Idea
A partner may engage in customary trade practices like bida'a, hiring, and mudaraba, but not lending partnership capital.

A partnership contract grants a partner certain implied authorities to facilitate trade. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq enumerates the permissible transactions that go beyond the specific trade. Understanding these permissions is essential for partners to avoid invalid transactions and potential liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actions mentioned
List the specific transactions: bida'a, hiring, deposit, mudaraba, agency. Note that lending is explicitly excluded.
These are supplementary to the core partnership business.
2
Understand the rationale
Each action is justified by custom (urf) and necessity in trade. The partnership contract implies these permissions.
Custom is a recognized source in Hanafi law.
3
Analyze the mudaraba detail
The profit distribution when a partner takes money as mudaraba depends on the context: if unrelated to partnership trade, profit is his alone; if related and done in partner's presence, it enters partnership; if done in partner's absence, profit is shared between the partner and mudarabah parties.
This shows the importance of intention and context.
4
Apply the istihsan exception
For lending, analogy (qiyas) imposes liability on the partner if capital is destroyed, but istihsan (juristic preference) exempts him.
Istihsan overrides qiyas in this case.
5
Synthesize the general principle
A partner has implied authority to engage in customary trade practices, but lending is not permitted. The Hanafi school balances legal reasoning with practical custom.
This principle guides modern partnership agreements.

Source Text

As for mudaraba; because by giving to the mudarib, the mudarib becomes a depositee, and by action an agent, and by profit a hired worker, and the partnership in it is necessary established by necessity of the other's entitlement to a share of the profit. So if he takes money as mudaraba to transact in what is not of their trade, then the profit is for him alone, because it did not enter under the partnership contract. Similarly, if he takes money as mudaraba in the presence of his partner to trade in what is of their trade. Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage lists permissible transactions that a partner may undertake with the partnership capital beyond the core partnership business. It begins with الإبضاع (bida'a): sending goods with a trader to sell, with all profit going to the capital owner. This is allowed because it is a customary practice. Similarly, the partner may hire an employee to buy and sell (استيجار), and may deposit money as a trust (وديعة). The text notes that lending (عارية) is not mentioned and thus not allowed; if a partner lends capital and it is destroyed, analogically he would be liable, but by istihsan he is not. For مضاربة (mudaraba), the partner may give partnership capital as a mudaraba investment. However, if the partner himself takes money as a mudaraba from others, the profit distribution depends on context: if the transaction is unrelated to the partnership's trade, the profit is his alone; if related and done in the presence of his partner, it becomes part of the partnership; if done in the partner's absence, it is shared between the partner and the mudarabah parties. Finally, the partner may appoint an agent (توكيل) as this is a necessary part of trade. These permissions are based on custom and necessity, reflecting the Hanafi school's practical approach.

Key Terms
إبضاع (ibḍāʻ): To send goods with a trader to sell, with profit for the capital owner.
استيجار (istījār): Hiring someone to sell and buy on behalf of the partnership.
مضاربة (muḍārabah): A profit-sharing partnership where one party provides capital and the other provides labor.
عارية (ʻāriyah): A loan of an object for use without compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The partner is definitely liable in all cases.
D) The partner is only liable if the borrower is a non-Muslim.

Key Takeaway
A partner may engage in customary supplementary transactions, but lending partnership capital is not permitted; however, if done, istihsan exempts from liability.

Purchase After Capital Loss: Shirkat Milk or Shirkat 'Aqd?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on partnership validity after capital loss

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between shirkat milk and shirkat 'aqd in the context of a partner's purchase after the other's capital loss, and apply the condition of explicit wakalah to determine joint ownership.

Core Idea
Explicit wakalah saves joint ownership; implicit wakalah falls with the partnership.

When one partner's capital is destroyed, the partnership contract is at stake. If the other partner then buys goods with his own money, a critical question arises: does the purchase belong to both or solely to the buyer? The Hanafi school has an internal dispute between al-Hasan ibn Ziyad and Imam Muhammad, with the resolution hinging on whether agency (wakalah) was explicitly stated. This lesson unpacks that dispute and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
One partner's property perishes. The other partner then purchases goods using his own capital. Determine the legal status of this purchase.
Focus on the moment after capital loss.
2
State the Two Positions
Al-Hasan ibn Ziyad holds that the purchase creates a partnership of ownership (shirkat milk). Imam Muhammad holds that the original contract of partnership (shirkat 'aqd) persists, allowing full disposal.
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Understand the Reasonings
Hasan: the partnership contract is invalidated by the destruction of one capital. Muhammad: the contract was validly formed and not retroactively voided.
The dispute is over the effect of subsequent loss.
4
Analyze the Resolution
If the partners explicitly stated wakalah in the partnership contract, that agency survives the dissolution. If only implicit wakalah (inherent in the partnership), it falls with the contract.
Explicit wakalah is a separate, surviving contract.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
With explicit wakalah: purchase is jointly owned. Without it: buyer owns it alone.
Practical difference in ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(عيني، فتح)

Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling from the matn regarding a scenario where one partner's property perishes, and then the other partner makes a purchase. The core issue is whether the purchase belongs to both partners or solely to the buyer. The commentator begins by explaining the literal meaning: if one partner buys an item using his own money, the item is jointly owned, and the buying partner can recover his share from the other. The key condition is whether the partners stipulated explicit agency (wakalah) in the partnership contract. The text then presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: Al-Hasan ibn Ziyad considers such a purchase to be a partnership of ownership (shirkat milk), meaning each can only dispose of his own share, because the original contract of partnership (shirkat 'aqd) was invalidated by the destruction of one partner's capital. Imam Muhammad, however, maintains that it remains a partnership contract (shirkat 'aqd), allowing each partner full disposal, because the contract was validly formed and the later destruction does not retroactively invalidate it. The commentator then gives the preferred ruling: if the partners explicitly stated wakalah, the purchase is shared; if they only had an implicit wakalah within the partnership, then the purchase belongs solely to the buyer, since the implicit wakalah falls with the partnership. This distinction ensures clarity: explicit wakalah stands independently.

Key Terms
شَرِكَة (shirkah): Partnership: a contract between two or more for joint commercial enterprise.
وَكَالَة (wakalah): Agency: authorization to act on behalf of another; here explicit wakalah survives the dissolution of the partnership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Only the buying partner
C) The partner whose property perished
D) It is invalid

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi partnership law, destruction of one partner's capital invalidates the partnership contract, but explicit wakalah can preserve joint ownership of later purchases.

Fixed Profit Condition in Partnership: Condition Void, Contract Valid
Condition of fixed profit invalidates the condition, not the partnership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a fixed profit condition in a partnership and explain why the condition is void while the partnership remains valid.

Core Idea
A fixed profit condition is void, but the partnership stands; profit is split by capital.

In partnership (shirkah), profit sharing must be based on a proportion of the profit, not a fixed amount. A common mistake is to assume that any corrupt condition destroys the entire contract. This lesson clarifies the Hanafi principle that a corrupt condition (fasid) does not necessarily invalidate the contract; only the condition is voided, and the contract operates as if it never existed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the stipulation
In the source text, one partner says, 'I will have 100 dirhams from the profit, then the remainder is divided.' This is a fixed amount, not a proportional share.
2
Classify the legal effect of the condition
The commentator (Ayni) rules that such a condition makes the partnership 'fasid' (corrupt). But note: he means the condition itself is fasid, not the entire partnership.
The word 'fasid' in Hanafi law is distinct from 'batil' (void). A fasid condition can be removed, leaving the contract intact.
3
Apply the Hanafi principle on corrupt conditions
Hanafi law holds that a corrupt condition does not invalidate the underlying contract if the contract can stand without it. Since the partnership's essence (sharing uncertain profit) remains, the condition is severed.
This is based on the maxim: 'Al-fasad fi al-shart la yufsid al-aqd' (Corruption in the condition does not spoil the contract).
4
Derive the ruling after the condition is voided
Once the fixed profit condition is removed, the partnership is governed by the default rule: profit is distributed according to each partner's capital contribution. This ensures fairness and avoids uncertainty.
This preserves the validity of the partnership while discarding the problematic stipulation.
5
Note the cross-madhhab disagreement
Some other madhhabs (e.g., Shafi'i) may consider such a condition to invalidate the entire partnership because they view the condition as integral to the contract. The Hanafi school, however, adopts a more flexible approach.
The practical difference: under Hanafi law, the partnership continues; under other views, it must be dissolved and reformed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسماة من الربح بأن قال أحدهما يكون لي من الربح مائة درهم مثلا ثم يقسم الباقي. (عيني) ] أي إذا شرط أحدهما دراهم معينة من الربح تفسد الشركة؛ لأنه شرط يوجب انقطاع الشركة؛ إذ قد لا يربح القدر الذي سمياه لأحدهما، وتنتفي بهذا الشرط الشركة فظاهر أن المراد فساد الشرط؛ لأن الشركة لا تبطل بالشروط الفاسدة فإذا بطل الشرط يكون الربح على قدر المال. (عيني، فتح)
Specified (dirhams) from the profit: that one of them says, 'I will have one hundred dirhams from the profit, for example, then the remainder is divided.' (Ayni) Meaning: if one of them stipulates specific dirhams from the profit, the partnership becomes corrupt (fasid); because it is a condition that necessitates the interruption of partnership, since it may be that they do not profit the amount they specified for one of them, and by this condition the partnership is negated. So it is apparent that what is meant is the corruption of the condition; because the partnership is not invalidated by corrupt conditions. Commentary
This passage clarifies a common point of confusion: what happens if partners stipulate a fixed amount of profit for one of them (e.g., 'I will have 100 dirhams of profit first, then we share the rest'). The commentator explains that such a condition renders the condition itself invalid (fasid), not the partnership. Why? Because a fixed profit contradicts the nature of partnership, which is based on sharing uncertain profit. Specifying a fixed amount may lead to a situation where the actual profit is less than that amount, thereby negating the partnership element. However, Hanafi law holds that invalid conditions do not nullify the underlying contract if the contract can stand without them. Thus, the condition is voided, and the profit is divided according to the capital ratio (i.e., each partner's capital share). This preserves the validity of the partnership while discarding the problematic stipulation.

Key Terms
فَسَاد (fasad): Corrupt but not void: a contract that is fundamentally valid but has a defective condition.
الشركة (al-shirkah): Partnership: a contract between two or more persons to share capital and profit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a Hanafi partnership, if one partner stipulates a fixed 100 dirham profit for himself before dividing the rest, what is the ruling?
A) The entire partnership is invalid
B) The condition is void, profit is divided according to capital
C) The condition is valid, the partner gets 100 dirhams first
D) The partnership becomes voidable at the other partner's option

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi partnership law, a stipulation of fixed profit is a corrupt condition that is voided, leaving the partnership valid and profit to be shared proportionally by capital.

Shirkah — Partnership

Permissible Customary Actions in Partnership: Bida'a, Ijarah, Wadi'a
List of permissible and prohibited actions for partners

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify bida'a, ijarah, and wadi'a as permissible actions for partners and explain why i'arah is not allowed, with the liability rule for i'arah by istihsan.

Core Idea
Partners may engage in customary trade contracts (bida'a, ijarah, wadi'a) but not gratuitous loans (i'arah), with liability for i'arah waived by istihsan.

In Hanafi partnership (shirkah), partners often need to use the partnership capital in various trade-related contracts. This lesson clarifies which common transactions are permissible and which are not. The key principle is that actions that are customary and necessary in trade are allowed, while non-trade acts like lending for free use (i'arah) are prohibited. Moreover, if a partner does lend and the property is lost, strict analogy would impose liability, but juristic preference (istihsan) exempts him.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Actions Listed
The matn states that each partner may 'yubdi' (give as bida'a), 'yasta'jir' (hire), 'yudi' (deposit), and 'yudarib' (enter mudaraba). First, focus on bida'a, ijarah, and wadi'a. Understand what each term means in the partnership context.
Bida'a: giving property to another to trade, profit to owner. Ijarah: hiring someone to buy/sell. Wadi'a: depositing property with another.
2
Connect to the Principle of Custom
The commentary explains these actions are 'customary actions from which a trader has no alternative.' Recognize that the permissibility is rooted in the necessity of trade, not explicit text. This is a form of istihsan (juristic preference) based on custom ('urf).
Custom plays a role in Hanafi fiqh as a source for permissibility when no explicit prohibition exists.
3
Identify the Prohibited Action: I'arah
The matn does not mention i'arah (lending for use). The commentator explicitly states a partner may not give an i'arah. Contrast this with the permissible actions. Understand that i'arah is a gratuitous contract, not a trade necessity.
I'arah is a loan of a fungible or non-fungible item for free use. It is not a trade transaction.
4
Analyze the Liability Rule for I'arah
The passage states: if a partner does give i'arah and the property is destroyed, by strict qiyas he would be liable (dhaman), but by istihsan he is not. This is a classic Hanafi application of istihsan overriding qiyas. Understand the reasoning: since the partnership capital is a trust (amanah) in the partner's hands, and i'arah is a misuse, strict liability is softened due to the necessity of trade? Actually, the commentary does not give a reason, but it's likely because the partner did not intend to harm and the act is not inherently negligent.
Qiyas: partner is liable for breach of trust. Istihsan: no liability because partnership capital is amanah and the partner is not a guarantor unless negligent.
5
Synthesize the Ruling
Compile the rulings: bida'a, ijarah, wadi'a are permissible; i'arah is not. If i'arah is done, the partner is not liable for loss without negligence. This shows the flexibility of Hanafi fiqh in accommodating trade customs.
These rulings are mufta bihi in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يبضع إلخ من الإبضاع وهو أن يدفع مالا لآخر ليتجر فيه ويكون الربح لرب المال؛ لأنه التجار. (عيني) ] أما دفع المال بضاعة واستيجاراً لمن يبيع ويشتري ودفع المال وديعة؛ فلأن هذه التصرفات معتادة لا يجد التاجر بداً منه. ولم يذكر الإعارة، فليس له أن يعير، فلو فعل وعطب المال، فالقياس أن يضمن، وفي الاستحسان لا، وأما المضاربة؛ فلأنه بالدفع إلى المضارب يصير المضارب مودعاً، وبالتصرف وكيلا، وبالربح أجيراً، والشركة فيه ضرورية تثبت ضرورة استحقاق الآخر من الربح مشاعاً. ومعنى قوله: "يضارب" أن يدفع المال مضاربة، وأما إذا أخذ المال مضاربة ففيه التفصيل، فإذا أخذ مالاً مضاربة ليتصرف فيما ليس من تجارتهما، فالربح له خاصة؛ لأنه لم يدخل تحت عقد الشركة، وكذلك إذا أخذ المال مضاربة بحضرة صاحبه ليتجر فيما هو من تجارتهما، وأما إذا أخذ المال مضاربة ليتصرف فيما كان تجارتهما من أو مطلقاً حال غيبة شريكه يكون الربح بينهما مشتركاً، نصفه لشريكه ونصفه بين المضارب ورب المال. وقوله: "ويوكل" أي له أن يوكل؛ لأن التوكيل من توابع التجارة والشركة انعقدت لها فله أن يفعل هذه الأشياء كلها - ويده في المال أمانة. (مستخلص، فتح، عيني)
(He may give as bida'ah) etc. from al-ibda', which is to give property to another to trade with, and the profit belongs to the owner of the property, because he is the trader. (Ayni) As for giving property as bida'ah, hiring someone to sell and buy, and giving property as a deposit (wadi'a), these are customary actions from which a trader has no alternative. And he did not mention giving as a loan (i'arah), so he may not give as a loan; if he does and the property is destroyed, by analogy (qiyas) he is liable, but by istihsan he is not. As for mudaraba, because by giving to the mudarib, the mudarib becomes a depository (wadi'), an agent (wakil) by disposal, and an employee (ajir) by profit; and partnership (shirkah) in it is necessary, established by necessity of the other's entitlement to a share of profit. And the meaning of his statement 'he enters into mudaraba' (yudarib) is that he gives the property as mudaraba. As for if he takes property via mudaraba, then there is detail: if he takes property via mudaraba to transact in what is not part of their trade, the profit is solely his, because it did not enter under the partnership contract. Similarly, if he takes the property via mudaraba in the presence of his partner to trade in what is part of their trade. But if he takes property via mudaraba to transact in what was part of their trade, or absolutely, during the absence of his partner, the profit is shared between them, half for his partner and half between the mudarib and the owner of the capital. And his statement 'and he may appoint a wakil' means he may appoint a wakil, because tawkili is among the accessories of trade, and the partnership was concluded for it, so he may do all these things - and his hand over the property is a trust (amanah). (Mustakhlas, Fath, Ayni)
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage explains the permissible actions that partners in 'inan or mufawadah partnerships may undertake. The matn states that each partner may 'yubdi' (give goods as bida'ah), 'yasta'jir' (hire), 'yudi' (deposit), 'yudarib' (enter into mudaraba), and 'yuwakkil' (appoint an agent). The commentary elaborates each: Bida'ah is giving property to another to trade, where profit goes to the owner. Ijarah, wadi'a, and mudaraba are all permissible because they are customary necessities for traders. I'arah (loan for use) is not mentioned and thus not allowed; if done and property is lost, strict qiyas would impose liability, but istihsan (juristic preference) absolves the partner. Mudaraba has a detailed rule: if a partner takes money as mudaraba to trade outside the partnership's scope, the profit is his alone; but if within the scope and in the partner's presence, the profit is shared? The text says if in the presence of his partner to trade in their own trade, then profit is between them; the exact wording is a bit garbled but the main point is that mudaraba by a partner is permitted with conditions. Finally, a partner may appoint an agent, as agency is ancillary to trade. The partner's hand over partnership capital is a trust (amanah), meaning he is not liable for loss without negligence.

Key Terms
إِبْضَاع (ibda'): Giving property to another to trade, with profit for the owner; similar to commenda but without profit-sharing.
أَجِير (ajir): Employee or hired person; here, the mudarib is considered an ajir by profit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following actions is NOT permissible for a Hanafi partner without explicit consent?
A) Bida'a
B) Ijarah
C) I'arah
D) Wadi'a

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi partnership, customary trade contracts (bida'a, ijarah, wadi'a) are permissible, but gratuitous loans (i'arah) are not, and liability for i'arah is waived by istihsan.

Shirkah — Partnership

Mudaraba and Agency in Partnership: Detailed Rules
Detailed rules for mudaraba and agency by a partner

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when a partner may engage in mudaraba, distinguish between giving and taking mudaraba, and explain the profit distribution rules when a partner acts as mudarib, as well as the permissibility of appointing an agent (wakil).

Core Idea
A partner may give mudaraba freely, but if he takes mudaraba, profit sharing depends on the trade scope and the partner's presence.

Building on the general permissibility of customary acts, this lesson focuses on mudaraba (commenda) and agency (wakala) in the context of partnership. While a partner can give partnership capital as mudaraba without restriction, taking mudaraba as a mudarib has detailed rules. If the partner takes mudaraba to trade outside the partnership's scope, the profit is his alone; if within the scope and in the presence of his co-partner, the profit is shared between them; and if within scope but in the absence of the co-partner, the profit is split half to the co-partner and half between the mudarib and the original capital owner. Additionally, a partner may appoint an agent because agency is accessory to trade.

Guided Path
1
Understand 'Yudarib' as Giving Mudaraba
The matn says 'yudarib' meaning the partner gives (pushes) the partnership capital as mudaraba to a third party. This is permissible without restriction. The mudarib becomes a depository (wadi'), agent (wakil), and employee (ajir) by profit. Recognize that this is a valid use of partnership capital.
The partner acts as rabb al-mal (investor) with a third party as mudarib.
2
Analyze the Case of Taking Mudaraba
The passage presents three scenarios when a partner takes mudaraba (i.e., acts as mudarib using someone else's capital, possibly including his own partnership capital?). Actually, the text says 'if he takes the property via mudaraba' meaning the partner receives capital as mudarib from an external person? Or from the partnership? The context: 'ifa akhadha al-mal mudaraba' could mean he takes the partnership capital as mudaraba for himself? Clarify: likely he takes external capital as mudarib while still a partner. Three cases: (1) trade outside partnership's scope -> profit solely for the partner; (2) trade within scope and in partner's presence -> profit shared? The text is ambiguous; (3) trade within scope and partner absent -> profit shared as described. Focus on the clear case (3).
Case (3): profit distribution: half to the co-partner (from partnership share), half split between mudarib (the acting partner) and the original capital owner (rabb al-mal).
3
Compare the Three Scenarios
Create a mental table: Scope (within/outside) and Presence (present/absent). Outside scope always gives profit to acting partner alone. Within scope: if present, profit shared between the two partners (as per partnership?); if absent, profit shared as in case (3). Note that this detail shows the Hanafi school's precision in balancing partnership rights.
The difference hinges on whether the partnership's business and the mudaraba overlap, and whether the co-partner is aware and can object.
4
Recognize the Permissibility of Tawkili
The matn states 'and he may appoint a wakil'. The commentary explains that agency is an accessory (tabi') of trade, and since the partnership was concluded for trade, a partner may delegate tasks. This is straightforward.
Tawkili includes all necessary agents, but the partner remains responsible.
5
Synthesize the Trust Status
The passage ends: 'and his hand over the property is a trust (amanah).' This applies to all the actions. The partner is not a guarantor unless he exceeds authority or is negligent. This is the overarching principle.
Amanah status is key: no liability for loss without negligence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
