(مستخلص، عيني) : ما للآخر أن أعانه بالغا ما بلغ وهو الصحيح الشركة الفاسدة التي يجوز أن تجعل صحيحة أي شركة تكون بقدر المال. (ط، عيني] لا يقال: إن في كلام المصنف تناقضاً؛ لأنه ذكر أولاً أن الكسب في الشركة الفاسدة للعامل، وعليه أجر مثل الآخر ثم ذكر ما يخالفه بقوله : " والربح" في الشركة الفاسدة بقدر المال وإن شرط الفضل"؛ لأن موضوع ما ذكره أولاً ما إذا وقعت الشركة في نحو الاستقاء من النهر، وموضوع ما ذكره ثانياً ما إذا وقعت الشركة في شراء البئر وبيعه مثلاً، واشترط الربح أثلاثاً مع التساوي في رأس المال، ولكن طرأ الفساد لأمر عارض كاشتراط تخصيص أحدهما من أصل الربح بدراهم مسماة. وأشار بقوله: "وإن شرط "الفضل" إلى أن جواز اشتراط التفاضل في الربح مع التساوي في رأس المال محله ما إذا صحت الشركة، أما إذا فسدت فلا، ويكون الربح بينهما بقدر المال حتى لو كان المال من أحدهما كان للآخر أجر المثل، ولأن الربح تابع للعقد إذا كان موجوداً، وههنا قد فقد العقد، فيكون تابعاً للمال، والزيادة إنما تستحق بالتسمية، وقد فسدت لفساد العقد، فصار كأن التسمية لم توجد أصلاً، فبطل شرط التفاضل وبقي الاستحقاق على قدر رأس المال. (عيني، فتح)
The defective partnership: and upon him is the hire-mithl of what belongs to the other; and the profit in a defective partnership is according to the capital even if a surplus was stipulated, i.e., on the worker; and the partnership becomes void by the death of one of them, even if constructively; and he does not pay zakat on the wealth of the other without his permission, i.e., the contract partnership. i.e., the other partner. And according to Abu Yusuf: he does not exceed half its value. Likewise, if they partner in drawing water, one having a mule and the other a water-skin to draw water on it, then whichever draws water has the hire for what he drew and upon him is the hire-mithl of the water-skin or the mule. And the hire-mithl is obligatory because he utilized the benefit of another by a defective contract, so he is entitled to the hire-mithl. (Mukhtalash, 'Ayni): That which is for the other, that he assisted him to the fullest extent, and it is correct that the defective partnership which can be made valid is a partnership according to capital. (T., 'Ayni) It should not be said that there is a contradiction in the author's words because he first mentioned that earnings in a defective partnership are for the worker and upon him is the hire-mithl of the other; then he mentioned what contradicts it by his saying: 'And the profit in a defective partnership is according to the capital even if a surplus was stipulated' because the subject of what he first mentioned is when the partnership occurs in matters like drawing water from the river; and the subject of what he second mentioned is when the partnership occurs in buying and selling a well, for example, and they stipulated profit in thirds while the capital is equal, but the defect arose from an incidental matter such as stipulating that one of them receive a specified amount of dirhams from the principal profit. And he indicated by his saying 'even if a surplus was stipulated' that the permissibility of stipulating disparity in profit while capital is equal applies only when the partnership is valid; but if it is defective, then not, and the profit between them is according to the capital, to the extent that if the capital is from one of them, the other has the hire-mithl; because profit follows the contract when it exists, but here the contract is absent, so it follows the capital, and the increase is only entitled by the stipulation, and it has become invalid due to the invalidity of the contract, so it is as if the stipulation never existed, so the condition of disparity becomes void and the entitlement remains according to the capital. ('Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (talil) on profit distribution in a defective (fasid) partnership. The author first states the general rule: in a fasid shirkah, the profit is distributed in proportion to the capital contributed, regardless of any stipulated condition for a larger share. He clarifies an apparent contradiction: earlier he said that in a fasid shirkah (like drawing water) the earnings belong to the worker and he must pay the other partner a hire-mithl for the use of his asset. This does not conflict with the later rule about profit in a fasid trade partnership (like buying and selling a well), because the first scenario involves a service partnership where the capital is a tangible asset (mule, water-skin) and the profit is the fee from the work; the second scenario involves capital investment where profit follows capital. The ruling is based on the principle that profit follows the contract, but if the contract is defective, profit follows the capital. The istihsan here is that the condition for excess profit is void because it was tied to an invalid contract, so entitlement reverts to capital proportion. The example of drawing water with a mule and water-skin illustrates that each party gets the hire for his own labor and owes a hire-mithl for the use of the other's property. This is a standard Hanafi ruling found in Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
شركة فاسدة (shirkah fasidah): A partnership that is fundamentally valid but becomes defective due to a condition that violates its essence, e.g., stipulating a fix sum from profit.
أجر المثل (ajr al-mithl): The fair market hire for the benefit of a property or service, used as compensation when no specific wage is contracted.
فضل (fadl): Surplus or excess profit stipulated beyond the capital proportion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a defective partnership (shirkah fasidah) involving capital from both parties, how is profit distributed if they stipulated a 70-30 split but contributed equal capital?
A) Profit is split 70-30 as stipulated.
B) Profit is split equally because the stipulation is void.
C) The worker gets all profit and pays the other hire-mithl.
D) Profit is distributed according to the capital proportion (50-50).

Key Takeaway
In a defective partnership (shirkah fasidah), any condition for excess profit is void, and profit is distributed according to capital; for service partnerships, the worker gets earnings but must pay a hire-mithl for using the other's asset.

Termination of Partnership by Death and Constructive Death
Ruling on termination of shirkah by death and apostasy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that partnership ends automatically upon actual or constructive death without knowledge, and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the pending apostasy period.

Core Idea
Partnership ends at death even without knowledge; apostasy with joining enemy territory is constructive death.

Partnership (shirkah) inherently includes agency (wakalah). Since agency ends at death by constructive dismissal, partnership also ends automatically. This lesson explains the absolute rule and its exception for constructive death via apostasy, including the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad).

Guided Path
1
Understand the basis of partnership termination
Recognize that partnership inherently includes agency (wakalah). Therefore, anything that voids agency also voids the partnership. Death is a constructive dismissal (azl hukmī) that voids agency without need for knowledge.
The ruling is absolute: the partnership ends regardless of whether the surviving partner knows of the death.
2
Distinguish actual death from constructive death
Actual death clearly terminates. Constructive death occurs when a partner apostatizes and joins the abode of war (dar al-harb) and the court rules that he has joined. This is treated like death for partnership purposes.
If no court ruling, the termination is suspended by consensus.
3
Examine the suspended period before ruling
If a partner apostatizes but the court has not yet ruled on his joining dar al-harb, the partnership is suspended. If he returns to Islam before the ruling, the partnership revives. If he dies or is killed, it terminates from that moment.
Consensus among the three imams on this point.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on the nature of the suspended period
Abu Hanifa held that the partnership does not become an 'inan partnership during the suspension; it remains fully terminated (or pending as a single entity). Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) held that it converts into an 'inan partnership, allowing shared profits from that period.
The mufta bihi is the view of the Sahibayn.
5
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If the active partner trades during the suspension and the apostate returns, the profits are shared proportionally according to the Sahibayn, but belong entirely to the active partner according to Abu Hanifa.
This difference affects real business dealings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتبطل الشركة إلخ: مطلقاً سواء علم الشريك بموت صاحبه أو لا ؛ لأن الشركة تتضمن الوكالة وهي بالموت؛ لأنه عزل حكمي، فإذا بطلت الوكالة بطلت الشركة؛ إذ لابد لها منها، بخلاف ما إذا فسخ الشركة في حالة يكون له الفسخ فيها بأن كان المال دراهم ودنانير يتوقف على علم الآخر لكونه عزلاً قصدياً. رمز الحقائق: ٤٧٠/١] (فتح) ولو حكماً: أي ولو كان الموت حكماً بأن ارتد أحدهما ولحق بدار الحرب وقضي بلحاقه، فإن لم يقض به توقف انقطاع الشركة إجماعاً، فإن عاد قبل الحكم بقيت، وإن مات أو قتل انقطعت، وهل تنقلب عناناً التوقف؟ نفاه الإمام وأثبتاه. (فتح)
And the partnership becomes void etc.: absolutely, whether the partner knew of his partner's death or not; because the partnership includes agency, and agency ends with death because it is a constructive dismissal. So when agency becomes void, partnership becomes void, since it cannot do without it. Unlike when he dissolves the partnership in a situation where he has the right of rescission, such as when the capital is dirhams and dinars, it depends on the knowledge of the other because it is a deliberate dismissal. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/470] (Fath) 'Even if constructively': i.e., even if the death is constructive, such as where one of them apostatizes and joins the abode of war and it is ruled that he has joined; if it is not ruled, the termination of partnership is suspended by consensus. If he returns before the ruling, it remains; if he dies or is killed, it terminates. And does it become a limited partnership ('inan) during the suspension? Imam [Abu Hanifa] denied it, but they [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] affirmed it. (Fath)
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on termination of partnership. It clarifies that death terminates the partnership absolutely, even without the surviving partner's knowledge, because partnership inherently includes agency (wakalah), and agency is voided by death by constructive dismissal. The exception is when one partner dissolves the partnership voluntarily; that requires knowledge because it is a deliberate act. Then the text addresses constructive death: if a partner apostatizes and joins enemy territory and a court rules his joining, the partnership ends. If no ruling, the partnership is suspended until he returns (then it continues) or dies (then it ends). There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa says the pending status does not automatically become a limited partnership ('inan), but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say it does. The relied-upon position is that it becomes 'inan to preserve some continuity. This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
حكمي (hukmī): Constructive or juridical death, e.g., apostasy deemed to sever ties like death.
عزل حكمي (azl hukmī): Constructive dismissal, where agency ends without explicit word, as by death.
عزل قصدي (azl qasdī): Deliberate dismissal, such as one partner voluntarily dissolving the partnership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A and B are in a partnership. B apostatizes and joins a non-Muslim army. A continues trading for a week before the court rules that B has joined. During that week, the partnership is:
A) Void from the moment of apostasy.
B) Suspended; if B returns before ruling, it revives.
C) Automatically becomes an 'inan partnership.
D) Continues as before until the ruling.

Key Takeaway
Partnership ends automatically upon death (actual or constructive); during pending apostasy, the partnership is suspended, and the mufta bihi is that it becomes an 'inan partnership for profit sharing.

Ownership of Slave Girl Purchased with Permission for Intercourse
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ownership when buying slave girl for intercourse with partner's permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding ownership of a slave girl bought with partnership permission and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Permission for intercourse implies a gift of the partner's share, making the buyer sole owner — no compensation.

In a Hanafi partnership (mufawada or 'inan), what happens when one partner gives permission to buy a slave girl for his personal intercourse? Imam Abu Hanifa and the two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ sharply. This lesson walks through their proofs and the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
Identify the act: one partner gives explicit permission to the other to purchase a slave girl for his own intercourse. The purchase is made from partnership funds.
2
Identify the ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa holds the buyer owns her solely without any payment to the partner. The Sahibayn hold the buyer must pay half the price to the partner.
The practical difference is called thamarah al-khilaf.
3
Follow Abu Hanifa's proof
By default, the purchase puts the girl into joint ownership. But the permission for intercourse implies a gift of the partner’s share because intercourse requires full ownership. Thus, the buyer becomes sole owner by gift.
This treats the permission as a hiba (gift).
4
Follow Sahibayn's proof
The purchase was exclusively for the buyer's benefit (intercourse), so ownership and liability rest on him. Analogous to food and clothing for personal use, which are not shared. The buyer must reimburse half the price.
They analogize (qiyas) to the exemption of personal necessities.
5
Determine the tarjih
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is Imam Abu Hanifa's view: sole ownership without compensation. The partner is considered to have gifted his share.
The seller may claim the full price from either partner in either view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أذن أحد المفاوضين بشراء أمة ليطأها، ففعل، فهي للآخر أي المشتري له بلا شيء. وأديا معاً: (الظاهر أن التقييد به اتفاقي؛ لأن أحد شريكي العنان الحكم فيه كذلك) أي إذا أذن أحد الشريكين أن يشتري جارية فيطأها ففعل المأذون له كذلك فالجارية للمشتري بغير شيء عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا: يرجع على شريكه بنصف الثمن؛ لأن الملك وقع له خاصة حتى حل له وطؤها والثمن بمقابلة الملك، فيكون عليه خاصة، وقد قضاه من مال مشترك فيرجع عليه صاحبه بحصته كما في ثمن الطعام والكسوة، وله: أن الجارية تدخل في ملكهما جرياً على مقتضى الشركة؛ إذ لا يملكان تغييره ثم الإذن يتضمن هبة نصيبه؛ لأن الوطء لا يحل إلا بالملك، فصار كما إذا اشترياها. ثم قال أحدهما للآخر: اقبضها لك، كانت هبة بخلاف طعام الأهل وكسوتهم؛ لأن ذلك مستثنى عن الشركة للضرورة، فيقع الملك له خاصة بنفس العقد، فكان مؤدياً ديناً عليه من مال الشركة، وللبائع أن يطالب بالثمن أيهما شاء على التقديرين [ رمز الحقائق: ٤٧٠/١] (مستخلص) ففعل أي المشتري أمة مثل ما أذن له فهي له أي للمأذون له بالشراء لا للشركة.
And if one of the partners in a mufawada partnership authorizes the purchase of a slave girl for intercourse, and he does so, then she belongs to the other i.e., the purchaser, without anything. And they both pay together: (It seems that the restriction is agreed upon; because the ruling for one partner in an 'inan partnership is likewise.) i.e., if one of the two partners authorizes that he buys a slave girl and has intercourse with her, and the authorized person does so, then the slave girl belongs to the purchaser without any compensation according to Abu Hanifa. And they [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] said: he recovers half the price from his partner, because ownership occurred specifically for him so that intercourse became lawful for him, and the price is in exchange for ownership, so it is specifically upon him, and he paid it from common wealth, so his partner recovers his share from him, like the price of food and clothing. And his proof [Abu Hanifa's] is that the slave girl enters into their joint ownership according to the requirement of the partnership, since they cannot change that; then the permission includes a gift of his share, because intercourse is not lawful except with ownership, so it becomes as if they have jointly bought her, then one says to the other, 'Take her for yourself' – that would be a gift. Contrary to food and clothing for the family, because that is exempted from the partnership due to necessity, so ownership occurs specifically to him by the contract itself, and he becomes as one paying a debt owed by him from partnership wealth. And the seller may claim the price from whichever he wants on both estimations. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/470] (Mukhtalash) 'So he did': i.e., the purchaser bought a slave girl like what he was authorized, so she belongs to him, i.e., to the authorized purchaser, not to the partnership.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the ownership of a slave girl purchased by one partner with permission from the other for intercourse. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the slave girl becomes the sole property of the purchasing partner without any compensation to the other. His reasoning: the purchase is made from partnership funds, so initially the girl enters joint ownership; however, the partner's permission for intercourse implies a gift of his share, since intercourse is only lawful with full ownership. Therefore, the permission is treated as a gift, making the purchaser the sole owner. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that the purchaser must pay back half the price to the partner because the girl was purchased for his exclusive benefit (intercourse), so ownership and liability for the price rest solely on him. They analogize to food and clothing for the family, which are exempted from partnership and paid personally. Abu Hanifa distinguishes: food and clothing are necessities exempt by custom, whereas the slave girl is not a necessity; thus the default partnership rule applies, but the permission operates as a gift. The relied-upon position is that of Imam Abu Hanifa. The seller can claim the price from either partner in either view.

Key Terms
مفاوضة (mufawadah): A full partnership where both partners have equal capital, equal profit, and equal agency for each other's actions (rarely practiced).
عنان ('inan): A limited partnership with unequal capital or restricted agency; each partner is agent for the other only in specific matters.
هبة (hiba): A gift; transfer of ownership without compensation.
ضرورة (darurah): Necessity; a situation that permits departure from default rules.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a Hanafi partnership, one partner gives permission to the other to buy a slave girl for his own intercourse. According to the relied-upon view, the slave girl belongs to:
A) Both partners equally, as purchased from partnership funds.
B) The purchasing partner, without any compensation to the other.
C) The partner who gave permission, as he authorized the purchase.
D) The purchasing partner, but he must pay half the price to the other.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi partnership, permission for personal use may imply a gift of the partner's share, leading to sole ownership without compensation, as held by Imam Abu Hanifa (mufta bihi).

Guarantee in Zakat Payment with Mutual Permission in Fasid Partnership
Ruling with commentary on guarantee

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on guarantee when partners mutually authorize zakat payment and differentiate Abu Hanifa's view from the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Paying another's zakat without actual need creates guarantee, even if unaware.

This lesson examines a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute about guarantee (daman) when partners in a void partnership (shirkah fasidah) authorize each other to pay zakat. The text from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents the ruling and its reasoning, highlighting differences between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, and notes a preferred riwaya from al-Ziyadat.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling according to Abu Hanifa for both simultaneous and consecutive payment scenarios.
The matn says: if both pay together, each guarantees; if consecutively, the second guarantees.
2
Identify the opponent's view
Recognize that the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) condition guarantee on knowledge of prior payment.
The Sahibayn require knowledge; without knowledge, no guarantee.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the Sahibayn's reasoning: the agent acted in good faith, so he is not a violator.
Opposing dalil: 'If he did not know, he is not liable.'
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa's response: the agent's act is a violation regardless of knowledge because the obligation was already fulfilled.
The principal's prior payment is considered in estimation, making the agent's payment superfluous.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
Note the practical difference and identify the relied-upon position (al-Ziyadat says no guarantee at all).
The text states the riwaya from al-Ziyadat is considered sahih (correct).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشركة الفاسدة
كتاب الشركة
فإن أذن كل واحد وأديا معاً ضمنا، ولو متعاقباً ضمن الثاني

وأديا معاً: أي لو أذن كل واحد منهما لصاحبه أن يؤدي زكاته، فأدى كل واحد منهما معاً . عن شریكه، ضمن كل واحد نصيب صاحبه مطلقاً ويتقاصان، فإن كان مال أحدهما أكثر يرجع بالزيادة، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما لا يضمن شيئاً إن لم يعلم فإن قيل: ينبغي أن لا يجب الضمان عنده إذا أديا معا؛ لعدم سبق أداء الموكل فلم يقع فعل الوكيل نقداً، قلنا: أداء الموكل إن لم يسبقه تحقيقاً فقد سبقه اعتباراً وتقديراً؛ لأن تصرف الموكل على نفسه أقرب من تصرف الوكيل، فيصير سابقاً معنى كالوكيل بالبيع مع الموكل إذا باعا وخرج الكلامان معاً، فإنه ينفذ بيع الموكل دون بيع الوكيل. (مستخلص) ضمن الثاني: أي إن أدى الأول ثم أدى الثاني ضمن الثاني للأول علم بأداء صاحبه أو لم يعلم عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا إن علم يضمن، وإلا فلا، كذا في "كتاب الزكاة"، وفي "الزيادات": لا يضمن مطلقا علم بأداء شريكه أو لم يعلم، وهو الصحيح، وعلى هذا الخلاف الوكيل بأداء الزكاة أو الكفارات إذا أدى الآمر بنفسه مع المأمور أو قبله أو أعتق لهما أنه مأمور بالأداء، وقد أتى به وله أنه أتى بغير المأمور به؛ لأن المأمور به إسقاط الفرض عنه، ولم يسقط به الفرض، فصار مخالفاً علم أو لم يعلم، بخلاف المأمور بقضاء الدين حيث لا يضمن بقضائه بغير علم بعد قضاء الآمر ؛ لأنه لم يخالف.
The void partnership. The book of partnership. If each one [partner] gave permission and both paid together, they are guarantors; and if consecutively, the second is guarantor. 'And both paid together': meaning if each one gave permission to his partner to pay his zakat, and each paid together for his partner, then each guarantees the share of his partner absolutely, and they offset each other; if one's wealth is more, he recovers the excess. This is according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two [companions], he does not guarantee anything if he did not know. If it is said: guarantee should not be obligatory according to him when they pay together, due to the lack of precedence of the principal's payment, so the agent's action did not occur as payment. We say: the principal's payment, if not preceded in reality, is preceded in consideration and estimation, because the principal's own disposal is closer than the agent's disposal, so he becomes prior in meaning, like the agent for sale with the principal when both sell and the two statements exit together: the principal's sale is executed, not the agent's. (Mukhtasar) 'The second is guarantor': meaning if the first paid then the second paid, the second guarantees the first whether he knew of his partner's payment or not according to Abu Hanifa. The two [companions] said: if he knew, he guarantees; otherwise not. Thus in the Book of Zakat. In al-Ziyadat: he does not guarantee at all, whether he knew of his partner's payment or not, and that is correct. And upon this difference is the agent for paying zakat or expiations: if the principal himself pays along with the agent, or before him, or he frees [a slave] for both: [the principal says] he was commanded to pay and has done it; but the agent says he did other than what was commanded, because what was commanded was dropping the obligation from him, and the obligation was not dropped by his action, so he became contrary, whether he knew or not. Unlike the one commanded to repay a debt, where he does not guarantee by repaying without knowledge after the principal's repayment, because he did not act contrary.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the chapter on void partnership (al-shirkah al-fasidah). It addresses a scenario where partners in a fasid partnership mutually authorize each other to pay their respective zakat. The matn states: if each gives permission and they pay simultaneously, each is a guarantor (damn) for the other's share; if they pay consecutively, only the second is guarantor. The commentary expands on this. For simultaneous payment, Abu Hanifa holds that each guarantees the other's share absolutely, and they settle accounts. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say there is no guarantee if the payer did not know the other had already paid. A potential objection is raised: logically, if both pay together, neither is prior, so how can guarantee arise? The response is that the principal's own payment is considered prior in legal estimation, based on analogy with a dual sale scenario. For consecutive payment, Abu Hanifa again says the second guarantees regardless of knowledge; the companions say only if he knew. A different riwaya from al-Ziyadat states no guarantee at all, and this is considered the relied-upon position. The principle is then extended to agents for zakat and expiations: if the principal pays himself, the agent is considered contrary and must repay, unlike the case of debt repayment where no guarantee applies because the agent's action is still valid.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Legal liability to compensate; here, one partner's obligation to repay the other for zakat paid on his behalf.
مأمور (ma'mur): One who is commanded or authorized; here, the partner who is authorized to pay the other's zakat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, what condition must exist for the second partner to be liable for guarantee when paying zakat after the first?
A) The second must have known that the first already paid.
B) The second must have paid from his own wealth.
C) The second must have received a written permission.
D) The second must have paid the exact same amount as the first.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that no guarantee arises when an agent pays zakat after the principal has already paid, regardless of knowledge.

Shirkah — Partnership

8 min
Ownership of Shared Fund Purchase with Permission: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ownership and compensation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on compensation when one partner in a fasid partnership buys a slave-girl with permission, and explain the legal reasoning behind each view.

Core Idea
Permission for a specific personal use implies a gift of the partner's share, negating compensation.

In a fasid partnership, when one partner authorizes the other to purchase a slave-girl for intercourse, the question arises: does that asset belong solely to the buyer, or must he compensate the partnership? This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq, with its commentary, presents the classical Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two main disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understanding the reasoning—especially the concept of implied gift (hiba) versus reimbursement for joint-fund use—sharpens your ability to apply analogous partnership rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and parties
Recognize that the case involves a fasid mufawada partnership where one partner gives explicit permission to the other to buy a slave-girl for a specific personal purpose (intercourse). The dispute is whether the buyer owes the partnership half the price.
The permission is key—it is not a general trading authorization.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his dalil
Abu Hanifa rules that the slave-girl belongs to the buyer alone, with no compensation. His proof: the slave-girl would normally be joint property, but the permission to have intercourse implies a gift (hiba) of the partner's share because intercourse requires full ownership.
The analogy: it is as if both bought her and then one gifted his share.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their dalil
The two companions hold that the buyer must reimburse half the price to the partnership. Their proof: the purchase used joint funds, and the exclusive benefit (intercourse) is for the buyer alone, so he must compensate, similar to buying food or clothing for the family.
They draw an analogy (qiyas) to other personal expenses from joint money.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the Sahibayn
Abu Hanifa distinguishes the slave-girl case from food/clothing: those are exempt from partnership due to necessity (darurah), so ownership vests immediately in the buyer. Here, the permission itself transforms the joint purchase into a gift, obviating compensation.
The distinction hinges on necessity vs. permission for personal pleasure.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under Abu Hanifa, the buyer pays nothing extra; under Sahibayn, he must pay half the price. The seller may demand the full price from either partner in both views.
The creditor's right remains unaffected; the dispute is between partners.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أذن أحد المفاوضين بشراء أمة ليطأها، ففعل، فهي للآخر
أي المشتري
أي
له بلا شيء.
الأمة
نفسه وعن
[رمز الحقائق: ٤٧٠] (فتح) وإن أذن: الظاهر أن التقييد به اتفاقي؛ لأن أحد شريكي العنان الحكم فيه كذلك أي إذا أذن أحد الشريكين أن يشتري جارية فيطأها ففعل المأذون له كذلك فالجارية للمشتري بغير شيء عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا: يرجع على شريكه بنصف الثمن؛ لأن الملك وقع له خاصة حتى حل له ،وطؤها والثمن بمقابلة الملك، فيكون عليه خاصة، وقد قضاه من مال مشترك فيرجع عليه صاحبه بحصته كما في ثمن الطعام والكسوة، وله: أن الجارية تدخل في ملكهما جرياً على مقتضى الشركة؛ إذ لا يملكان تغييره ثم الإذن يتضمن هبة نصيبه؛ لأن الوطء لا يحل إلا بالملك، فصار كما إذا اشترياها.
للآخر
ثم قال أحدهما للآخر: اقبضها لك، كانت هبة بخلاف طعام الأهل وكسوتهم؛ لأن ذلك مستثنى عن الشركة للضرورة، فيقع الملك له خاصة بنفس العقد، فكان مؤدياً ديناً عليه من مال الشركة، وللبائع أن يطالب بالثمن أيهما شاء على التقديرين [ رمز الحقائق: ٤٧٠/١] (مستخلص)
ففعل أي المشتري أمة مثل ما أذن له فهي له أي للمأذون له بالشراء لا للشركة.
And if one of the partners in a mufawada partnership gives permission to buy a slave-girl to have intercourse with her, and he does so, then she belongs to the other. Meaning the buyer. Meaning he owns her for nothing. The slave-girl. Himself and from. [Reference: 470] (Fath) 'And if he gave permission': it is apparent that the restriction is coincidental, because the ruling for one of the partners in an inan partnership is similar: i.e., if one of the two partners gives permission to buy a slave-girl to have intercourse with her, and the authorized person does so, then the slave-girl belongs to the buyer for nothing according to Abu Hanifa. The two [companions] said: he recovers from his partner half the price, because ownership occurred specifically for him such that intercourse became permissible; the price is in exchange for ownership, so it is upon him specifically, and he paid it from joint property, so his partner recovers his share from him, like the price of food and clothing. And [Abu Hanifa's] proof: the slave-girl enters into their joint ownership according to the requirement of partnership, since they cannot change that; then the permission implies a gift of his share, because intercourse is only permissible through ownership; so it is as if they both bought her. 'To the other': then if one of them says to the other: take her for yourself, it is a gift, unlike food for family and their clothing, because that is exempted from the partnership due to necessity, so ownership occurs specifically for him by the contract itself, and thus he is paying a debt owed by him from partnership property. The seller may demand the price from whichever of them he wishes according to both views. [Reference: 470/1] (Mukhtasar) 'And he did': i.e., the buyer bought a slave-girl as he was authorized, so she belongs to him, i.e., to the authorized buyer, not to the partnership.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling concerning a void (fasid) or mufawada partnership where one partner authorizes the other to purchase a slave-girl specifically for intercourse. The matn states: if permission is given and the authorized partner buys, the slave-girl belongs to the buyer (the other partner is not a co-owner). The commentary from Fath al-Qadir explains that this is the position of Abu Hanifa: the slave-girl is the buyer's alone, and he owes nothing to the partnership. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree, saying the buyer must compensate his partner with half the price, because the purchase was from joint funds and the benefit (intercourse) is exclusive to the buyer. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that the slave-girl would normally enter joint ownership by the nature of the partnership, but the permission to have intercourse implies a gift of the partner's share, since intercourse is only lawful when the man has full ownership. Therefore, it is as if both bought her, then the partner gifted his share. The commentary then distinguishes this from buying food or clothing for the family, which is exempt from partnership due to necessity, so ownership vests immediately in the buyer alone. The seller may demand the full price from either partner in both views.

Key Terms
مفاوضة (mufawada): A type of partnership where partners have equal rights and liabilities; here it is a void (fasid) form.
هبة (hiba): Gift; here, the implied gift of the partner's share in the slave-girl to the buyer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, when one partner buys a slave-girl with the other's permission for intercourse, why does the buyer not owe anything to the partnership?
A) Because the permission is void.
B) Because the permission implies a gift of the partner's share.
C) Because the slave-girl is for the partnership anyway.
D) Because the buyer acted without authority.

Key Takeaway
In a fasid partnership, permission for a personal act like intercourse can imply a gift of the partner's share, eliminating the need for compensation under Abu Hanifa's view, but the Sahibayn require reimbursement.

Shirkah — Partnership
intermediate
8 min
Rights Attach to Agent in Self-Added Contracts
Explains that when an agent adds a contract to himself, rights attach to agent, not principal, with proofs and exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that rights attach to the agent when he adds the contract to himself, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
When an agent adds a contract to himself, all rights and obligations attach to him, not the principal.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi principle in agency law: the distinction between an agent who attributes the contract to himself (e.g., 'I sell') and one who acts as a mere messenger. The Hanafi school holds that in the former case, the agent becomes personally liable for all contractual obligations, contrary to the Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali schools. We will explore the proofs, the exception for interdicted agents, and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
Recognize that the agent says 'I sell' or 'I lease' without mentioning the principal. According to Hanafi fiqh, rights like delivery, warranty, and payment attach to the agent.
This is the default rule unless the agent is a minor or slave (interdicted).
2
State the Hanafi proof
The agent is the direct origin of the contract; he does not need to attribute it to the principal. The other party relies on the agent's liability. If rights went to the principal, the counterparty could be harmed if the principal is insolvent or absent.
This is based on consideration of harm (darar) and the agent's independence.
3
Contrast with opposing view
Al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad argue that rights follow the judgment (hukm), so the principal is the real owner and should be liable. They analogize the agent to a messenger or marriage agent.
The Hanafi rebuttal distinguishes the agent's self-attributed contract from a messenger's role.
4
Note the exception for interdiction
If the agent is a minor or slave (interdicted), rights attach to the principal to prevent harm, because the agent cannot validly assume liability due to deficient capacity.
This exception harmonizes with the rule that liability requires full legal capacity.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if the agent sells an item, the buyer can sue the agent personally for delivery or refund. Under the opposing view, the buyer would have to sue the principal.
This affects litigation strategy and risk allocation in trade.

Source Text

والحقوق فيما يضيفه الوكيل إلى نفسه كالبيع والإجارة والصلح ف زك جمع حق مبتدا عند العقد عن إقرار تتعلق بالوكيل إن لم يكن محجوراً كتسليم المبيع. = خيرا المبتدأ دون المؤكل الوكيل عليه بصغر أو رق مع لما في ذلك من ضرب شبهة كما في كتاب القاضي إلى القاضي والشهادة على الشهادة، وشهادة النساء الرجال؛ لأن في صورة غيبة الموكل احتمال العفو ثابت فإن العفو مندوب إليه، بخلاف ما إذا حضر الموكل؛ لانتفاء هذا الاحتمال وقال الشافعي يستوفى القصاص في حال غيبة الموكل؛ لأنه حق العبد، ولنا: أنه عقوبة، فسقط بالشبهة، وشبهة العفو ثابتة في حال غيبة الموكل بل هو الظاهر؛ إذ العفو مندوب إليه، ويجوز التوكيل بإثبات القصاص وحد القذف والسرقة بإقامة ،البينة، فإذا أقيمت وثبت الحق، فللموكل استيفاءها. وقال أبو يوسف: لا يجوز بإثباتها أيضاً كما لا يجوز باستيفائها، وعلى هذا الخلاف التوكيل بالجواب من جانب من عليه الحد، فإذا وكل من عليه الحد والقصاص رجلاً بالجواب عنه، قال أبو حنيفة: يجوز، وقال أبو يوسف: لا يجوز، وقول محمد مضطرب، غير أن إقرار الوكيل لا يقبل عليه؛ لأن فيه شبهة عدم الأمر به، ولا يخفى أن قصر الاستثناء على الحد ،والقود يشعر بصحة التوكيل بإثبات التعزير. (عيني، فتح، عناية) غاب الموكل: لأنهما يندرءان بالشبهة، واحتمال العفو ثابت للندب إليه. (عيني) يضيفه: أي الكائنة في الشيء الذي يضيفه إلخ. (عيني) تتعلق بالوكيل وعند الشافعي تتعلق بالموكل وبه قال مالك وأحمد؛ لأن الحقوق تبع للحكم، والوكيل ليس بأصل فيه فلا يكون أصلا فيها، فصار كالرسول والوكيل بالنكاح، ولنا: أن الوكيل أصل في العقد بدليل استغنائه عن إضافته إلى الموكل ولو كان سفيرا كما زعم لما استغنى، وإنما جعل نائبا في الحكم للضرورة؛ كي لا يبطل مقصود الموكل، ولأن العاقد الآخر اعتمد رجوع الحق إليه، فلو لم يرجع إلى الوكيل لتضرر الآخر على تقدير كون الموكل مفلساً، أو من لا يقدر على مطالبته واستيفاء الثمن منه، بخلاف الرسول؛ فإنه يضيف العقد إلى مرسله وبخلاف النكاح؛ لأنه لابد فيه من ذكر الموكل وإسناد العقد إليه، فلا ضرر حينئذ. رمز الحقائق: ١٦٨/٢] إن لم يكن محجوراً: عبداً كان المحجور أو صبياً؛ فإنهما إذا عقدا بطريق الوكالة يتعلق الحقوق في عقديهما بالموكل دفعاً للضرر عن العاقد معهما؛ إذ لا يصح من المحجور التزام العهدة؛ لقصور أهليته؛ ولحق مولى العبد، كما في الرسول والقاضي وأمينه، وهذا يشير إلى أن الصبي والعبد المأذون لهما تتعلق بهما الحقوق، وتلزمها العهدة، وظاهر كلام المصنف أن العهدة على المأذون مطلقا، وفصل في "الذخيرة" بين أن يكون وكيلا بالبيع فالعهدة عليه، سواء باع بثمن حال أو مؤجل، وبين أن يكون وكيلا بالشراء، فإن كان بثمن مؤجل فهو على الموكل؛ لأنه في معنى الكفالة، وإن كان بثمن حال فهو على الوكيل؛ لكونه ضمان ثمن. (عيني، فتح) كتسليم: مثال للحقوق التي يتعلق بالوكيل فيما يضيفه إلى نفسه، فإن تسليم المبيع يجب على الوكيل إذا كان بائعا. (عيني)
And rights in what the agent adds to himself like sale, lease, and compromise... so that rights attach to the agent unless he is under interdiction by minority or slavery, like delivery of the sold item... because in that there is a doubt (shubha) as in the letter of a judge to another judge, and testimony upon testimony, and women's testimony with men; because in the case of the principal's absence, the probability of pardon exists, for pardon is recommended. Contrary to when the principal is present, because this probability is absent. Al-Shafi'i said: Qisas is executed in the absence of the principal because it is a right of the slave. Our proof: it is a punishment, so it is dropped by doubt, and the doubt of pardon is established in the absence of the principal, indeed it is apparent, since pardon is recommended. Agency for proving qisas, hadd of slander and theft by presenting evidence is permissible; if evidence is presented and the right is established, the principal may execute it himself. Abu Yusuf said: it is not permissible for proving them either, just as it is not permissible for executing them. And on this difference is agency for answering on behalf of the one upon whom the hadd is due. So if the one upon whom hadd or qisas is due appoints a man to answer on his behalf, Abu Hanifa said: it is permissible, and Abu Yusuf said: it is not permissible. And the saying of Muhammad is inconsistent, but the agent's confession is not accepted against him because there is a doubt about his authorization. And it is not hidden that restricting the exception to hadd and qisas indicates the validity of agency for proving ta'zir. ('Ayni, Fath, 'Inayah) 'If the principal is absent': because they (hadd and qisas) are averted by doubt, and the probability of pardon exists due to its recommendation. ('Ayni) 'Adds to himself': i.e., rights that are in the thing which he adds etc. ('Ayni) 'Attach to the agent': according to al-Shafi'i, they attach to the principal, and Malik and Ahmad said likewise, because rights follow the judgment, and the agent is not the origin in it, so he is not origin in them; so he is like a messenger and the agent in marriage. Our proof: the agent is an origin in the contract, evidenced by his independence from adding (the contract) to the principal. If he were a messenger as they claim, he would not be independent. He is only made a deputy in judgment by necessity, so that the principal's purpose is not nullified. And because the other contracting party relied on recourse to him (the agent) for the right. If the right did not revert to the agent, the other party would be harmed if the principal is bankrupt or cannot be demanded payment. Unlike the messenger, who adds the contract to his sender. And unlike marriage, because it is necessary to mention the principal and attribute the contract to him, so no harm then. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/168] 'Unless he is under interdiction': whether the interdicted is a slave or a minor; for when they contract by way of agency, rights in their contracts attach to the principal to prevent harm to the contracting party; because it is not valid for an interdicted person to assume liability due to deficiency of capacity, and due to the rights of the slave's master, as in the case of a messenger, judge, and his trustee. And this indicates that the minor and the authorized slave have rights attach to them and liability is upon them. The apparent meaning of the author's statement is that liability is on the authorized agent absolutely. But al-Dhakhira made a distinction: if he is an agent for sale, liability is upon him whether he sells for prompt or deferred payment; if he is an agent for purchase, if the payment is deferred, it is upon the principal because it is in the nature of a guarantee; if prompt, it is upon the agent because it is a guarantee of the price. ('Ayni, Fath) 'Like delivery': an example of rights that attach to the agent in what he adds to himself; delivery of the sold item is obligatory upon the agent if he is the seller. ('Ayni)
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage addresses the legal principle that when an agent acts in a contract that he adds to himself (yudifu ila nafsihi), such as saying 'I sell' without naming the principal, the rights and obligations of the contract attach to the agent, not the principal. This is the Hanafi position, contrary to al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad, who hold that rights attach to the principal. The Hanafi proof: the agent is the origin in the contract because he does not need to add the principal; he acts independently. The other contracting party relies on the agent personally; if rights did not attach to the agent, the other party would be harmed if the principal is insolvent or absent. However, if the agent is under interdiction (minor or slave), rights attach to the principal to prevent harm. The passage also discusses a distinction from al-Dhakhira regarding liability in sale vs. purchase agency. An example given is delivery of the sold item: the agent (seller) is obligated to deliver. The latter part of the passage (which overlaps with the previous lesson) repeats the hadd/qisas discussion but is included here for completeness. The focus is on the principle of rights attachment.

Key Terms
يضيفه إلى نفسه (yudifuhu ila nafsihi): The agent attributes the contract to himself (e.g., 'I sell') rather than 'I sell for my principal'.
عهدة (uhdah): Liability or responsibility for the contract, including warranty and delivery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if an agent sells an item saying 'I sell' without mentioning his principal, to whom does the obligation to deliver the item attach?
A) To the principal
B) To the agent
C) To both equally
D) To the judge

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an agent who adds a contract to himself bears full contractual liability, protecting the counterparty and reflecting the agent's independent role.
